## Page 69

to generate a reliable model of buried topographic landforms, and identify the paleosol(s) or buried surface(s) associated with the cultural deposits identified in the cutbank of the Trinity River. Five trackhoe trenches (Trenches 1 through 4, and 14) were excavated at site 41DL318 (Figure 7). Each trench measured between 5 and 8 m long and was excavated to a minimum depth of 2 m below surface (bs) (Table 4). Trench 1 was positioned perpendicular to the cutbank of the Trinity River. Trench 2 was approximately 20 south of Trench 1, near the channel of Honey Springs Branch on the south side of the site. Trench 3 was placed on the east side of the site, near Honey Springs Branch, which separates 41DL318 from 41DL319/357, The excavation of Trench 4 was intended to identify the western edge of the site, but its placement was limited by the extensive disturbance in this area due to construction of the 1-45 bridge over the Trinity River. Finally, Trench 14 was placed midway between Trench 1 and Trench 2, in the approximate center of the site. All five trenches excavated at site 41DL318 produced small amounts of shell. Four trenches (1, 2, 3, and 14) also produced small fragments of animal bone (including a tooth fragment from Trench 1, 190-200 cm bs). Three of trenches (1, 3, and 4) contained small flecks of burned clay mixed into a buried soil; and three trenches (1, 2, and 3) produced a small number of burned rocks (Table 5). This material was recovered from depths ranging from 100 cm bs to a maximum of 310 cm bs (bone). No significant concentrations of shell, burned clay, or burned rock were identified, and no features or artifacts were discovered during the trenching of site 41DL318. Shell quantities were limited—the most productive level was between 200 and 210cm bs in Trench 1, which produced approximately 13 g of shell. Subsequent to trenching, hand-excavated units were placed adjacent to Trenches 1 and 2. The noncultural overburden on the east side of Trench 1 was removed and a 1-x-.5-m unit (Unit 1) was started at a depth of 70 cm bs. Similarly, a 1-x-.5-m unit (Unit 2) was started on the east 51 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites side of Trench 2 at 60 cm bs. Materials recovered in Unit 1 consisted primarily of small flecks of charcoal from 70 cm to 140 cm bs, shell fragments between 110 cm and 200 cm bs, and small bone fragments between 80 and 130cm bs. A_ single feature (Feature 3) consisting of a fine, grayish, chalk-like ashy substance was encountered in Unit 1 and will be discussed in detail below. Unit 2, excavated on the east side of Trench 2, was started at a depth of 60 cm bs. Materials recovered in Unit 2 consisted primarily of small flecks of charcoal from 70 cm to 150 cm bs, small amounts of shell between 150 cm and 220 cm bs, and small bone fragments between 60 and 180 cm bs. Generally, recovery from the two units excavated at site 41DL318 was similar in the type and quantity of material recovered, although no features were identified in Unit 2. Small isolated fragments (ca. 1-2 mm in size) of burned clay were recovered in Unit 2, between 140 and 220 cm bs; however, no concentrations of this material were identified. As in Unit 1, shell quantities were relatively limited. Sediment and Stratigraphy The soil on site 41DL318 is mapped as Trinity clay, frequently flooded (USDA, SCS 1980). The site itself is located directly south of a meander loop of the Trinity River, between the mouth of Honey Springs Branch and the I-45 overpass. Unlike the other sites within the project area, site 41DL318 does not appear to have been subjected to a high degree of human disturbance. The only potential disturbance is that associated with the construction of the I-45 overpass; however, due to the depth of the site, disturbances related to the highway have probably not affected it. The identification of the site in the cutbank of the Trinity River does, nonetheless, indicate that erosion has affected it, although to what degree is not known. In the month that the field crew was in the area testing sites 41DL318 and 41DL319/357, flooding caused the erosion of approximately 0.3 m of soil along the edge of the site.

## Page 72

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas Trenching at site 41DL318 revealed sediments characterized by a C-2A-3Ab (or Abca)-3Bbca- 3Cgea profile. Profiles are illustrated in Figures 8 (northwest to southeast) and 9 (southwest to northeast) and detailed in Appendix F. The C horizon consists of approximately 15 cm of recent fluvial deposits. This horizon consists of brown to pale brown (10YR 5/3 to 6/3) primarily loose, fine-grained, silty sand with abundant woody roots and rootlets. These fluvial deposits are consistently 15 cm thick across the site and the boundary is clear to abrupt. The presence of yellow “Caution” tape in the upper 10 cm in Trench 4 is a clear indication of disturbance of the western area of the site, probably related to the construction of the I-45 overpass. The 2A horizon appears to represent a very late Holocene alluvium with an accumulation of humified organic matter and often exhibits evidence of disturbance. This horizon is characterized by a dark gray to grayish brown ClOYR 4/1 to 5/2) clay with a firm, blocky structure and clay skins on ped faces. Fine, sand-filled cracks or root casts are common, particularly in the upper portion. In some areas (i.e., Trenches 1 and 4), darker clay from the underlying horizon was observed, giving the 2A horizon a mottled appearance. Plastic fragments were common in the upper portions of Trenches 2 and 14; however, the lower portion of the horizon appeared intact. The boundary of this horizon is gradual to clear and is marked by a few rounded gravels and small limestone fragments. It dips gently to the north and south, which suggests that the area of the site was once on a slightly elevated ridge between the Trinity River and Honey Springs Branch. The 2A horizon ranges in thickness across the site from approximately 80 cm in Trenches | and 4 near the edge of the cutbank to approximately 95 cm in Trench 2, 20 m south of the cutbank. The 2A horizon was absent in Trench 3. Instead, a slightly darker 2C horizon extended to a depth of 185 to 200 cm and revealed evidence of 54 disturbance in the form of abundant plastic and copper wire fragments. The boundary of this 2C horizon was gradual and, as with the other trenches, dipped slightly to the north and was marked by a few rounded gravels. The presence of moderately coarse clastics in this historic alluvium overlying the West Fork soil suggests that deposition of the West Fork ceased when the Trinity River established its current meander belt. These clastics, which coarsen upwards, seem to represent deposition in natural levees along the current channel margin (see Appendix F). The West Fork soil appears as a thick, very dark, clay-rich cumulic soil that grades very slowly into its parent material, the Pilot Point alluvium. It was identified at depths ranging from approximately 95 cm in Trenches 1 and 4 to 150 cm in Trenches 2 and 14 (see Appendix F). The West Fork soil is subdivided into A, B, and C horizons. While the present topography is relatively flat from north to south, and even slightly elevated away from the cutbank, the trench profiles indicate the West Fork paleosurface was elevated nearer the river channel. The nature of the West Fork soil itself suggests slow deposition of fine grained clastics common to the back swamp portions of an aggrading flood plain, although the location of the river channel itself at that time is unknown. The A horizon of the West Fork soil (designated 3Ab or 3Abca in Figures 8 and 9 and in Appendix F) consists of very dark gray (JOYR 3/1) clay with a strong, blocky structure. Common characteristics include calcareous deposits along rootlet casts; small to large, vertical, sand- or clay-filled pockets, particularly in the upper zone of the horizon; and occasional decaying organic matter. The thickness of the 3A horizon ranged from 45 cm in Trenches 2, 3, and 14, located at the eastern and southern edges of the site, to approximately 105 cm in Trench 1, located at the edge of the cutbank.

## Page 75

The B horizon of the West Fork soil (3Bbca in Figures 8 and 9 and in Appendix F) is characterized as a very dark grayish brown to dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2 to 4/2) clay with coarse to fine blocky structure. Clay coats are common on ped faces, while silt and sand coats were also observed. An increase in the amount of calcareous concretions from the 3A horizon was also noted. Although the 3B horizon itself was reached in all five trenches excavated at site 41DL318, the maximum depth of this horizon was confirmed in only three (Trenches 1, 2, and 14) of the five trenches. The 3B horizon ranged in thickness from 35 cm in Trench 1 to 205 cm in Trenches 2 and 14. The combined thickness of the 3A and 3B horizons is relatively consistent across the site from north to south, ranging from 140 to 150 cm. The parent material of the West Fork soil 3Cca in Figures 8 and 9 and appendix F) consists of unaltered Pilot Point alluvium. It is characterized by dark grayish brown to brown (10YR 4/2 to 5/3) clay with a fine blocky structure. Clay skins are present on some ped faces and calcareous concretions are abundant. The 3C horizon was identified in Trenches 1, 2, and 14, starting at depths ranging from 250 cm (Trench 1) to 300 cm bs (Trenches 2 and 14). The West Fork soil is underlain by earlier alluvial deposits of indeterminate age. Possibly they are of middle or early Holocene date, but they were exposed only at the base of Trench 1 and along the’cutbank of the Trinity River. This horizon (4Cgca in Figure 8 and Appendix F) is characterized by gray (10YR 5/1) clay, with common fine calcareous concretions, and exhibits evidence of gleying represented by orange mottling. In Trench 1, this horizon was identified at a depth of 385 cm bs. Horizontal and Vertical Extent Based on the excavation of five trenches, all of which were positive, and the mussel shell observed in the cut bank of the Trinity River, site 57 Chapter 5: Resulis of Evaluations of Eight Sites 41DL318 measures approximately 75 m northwest-southeast by 50 m_ northeast- southwest and covers an area of approximately 3,750 m? (see Figure 7). Although these dimensions should bé viewed as approximations because of the limited number of trenches that were excavated, the area of the site was restricted by the cutbank along the Trinity River to the north, by Honey Springs Creek to the south and east, and by the disturbances related to the construction of the I-45 overpass on the west. No mussel shell was observed eroding from the cutbank along Honey Springs Branch. Trenches 1 through 4 were excavated early in the testing program and determined the placement of Units | and 2. Trench 14 was excavated later in the middle of the site, primarily to test the potential of this area to produce features. All five of the trenches excavated at site 41DL318 produced shell remains. The most intensive utilization appears to be located in the area of Trenches 1, 2, and 14 in the middle of the site (see Figure 7). Table 6 summarizes the recovery from each of the trench excavations and shows that Trenches 1, 2, and 14 yielded 90 percent (148.8 g) of all shell and 92 percent (23.2 g) of all bone by weight. Trench 3 produced the most burned clay (by weight), although in general very little burned clay was recovered from any of the trenches. The only lithic material recovered as a result of the trenching at site 41DL318 consisted of six burned rocks from Trenches 1, 2, and 3, none of which were associated with cultural features. Although chipped stone was recovered from site 41DL3 18, it was found only in the flotation sample from Feature 3, a concentration of ash-like material identified in Unit 1 (see below). Units 1 and 2 were placed adjacent to Trenches 1 and 2, respectively. Artifact and ecofact recovery from each of these units is summarized by depth in Table 7. Both units were started at similar depths and spanned the same soil horizons—the West Fork soil was encountered below 130 to 140 cm bs. Unit 1 yielded significantly more shell, bone, and charcoal than

## Page 77

did Unit 2, although only Unit 2 produced burned clay. The greater quantity of charcoal in Unit 1 was probably due to the presence of Feature 3 (see below). Both units are discussed in detail below. When standardized density figures (per m°) are examined for shell, bone, and charcoal for the two units (Table 8), the density of all three materials is significantly greater in Unit 1 than in Unit 2. The greater density of charcoal in Unit 1 is likely due to the presence of Feature 3, since 68 percent (5.1 g) of the charcoal from Unit I was recovered from the same depth as the feature. The higher density of shell, however, is not simply due to the presence of the feature since only 29 percent (5.3 g) of the shell from Unit | was recovered from the same level as the feature. Overall, the vertical distribution of shell, charcoal, and baked clay in Units 1 and 2 show concentrations of material between 130 and 150 cm bs (shell in Unit 1 and charcoal in Units 1 and 2), and between 180 and 200 cm bs (shell in Unit 1 and shell and burned clay in Unit 2; see Table 7). Since only two units were excavated, care should be taken when drawing conclusions regarding the horizontal distribution of material and how that relates to prehistoric activity areas. Nonetheless, the increase of shell, charcoal, and bummed clay at the same depths in both units strongly suggests two distinct periods of prehistoric utilization of the site. Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites The general distribution of artifacts and ecofacts in both the trenches and the units, and their relationship to the identified soil horizons, is illustrated in the cross sections presented in the previous subsection (see Figures 8 and 9). It is clear from these figures that most of the remains were recovered from the West Fork soil (Ab, Abca, and Bbca soil horizons). This is particularly true of shell and burned clay fragments, which were recovered almost exclusively from the West Fork. Charcoal and bone fragments appear to be slightly more scattered. The arbitrary excavation levels that correspond to the West Fork in both the units and trenches are presented in bold in Tables 5 and 7. This information has been summarized in Table 9 for the units (excluding floated material from Feature 3). Table 9 shows that 98.8 percent of the shell (23.9 g), 100 percent of the burned clay (8.2 g), 59.8 percent of the charcoal (5.2 g), but only 3.7 percent of the bone were recovered from either the A or B horizon of the West Fork soil. Furthermore, 98.3 percent of the shell and 93.9 percent of the burned clay was recovered from the West Fork A horizon. The few remains recovered from the B horizon of the West Fork and the 2A horizon above the West Fork are probably the result of postdepositional vertical migration due to bioturbation. Unit 1 was placed on the east side of Trench 1 and excavation was begun at 70 to 80 cm bs. The upper levels of the unit correspond to the 2A zone discussed above—a dark to very dark grayish brown (LOYR 4/2 to 3/2) silty clay with gray clay-filled krotovina (Figure 10). The West Fork soil was encountered at the same depth as the ash-like substance of Feature 3, at a depth of 130 cm bs, Approximately 30 percent (5.3 g) of the shell and 68 percent (5.1 g) of the charcoal recovered from Unit 1 was recovered from the same levels as Feature 3 (130 to 150 cm bs; see Table 7). The ashy sediment from Feature 3 was submitted for radiocarbon analysis and produced a date of 1,000 + 50 years B.P. (Beta -129633; organic sediment; 6 °C = -21.4%o). Another increase in the amount of shell was noted between 180 and 200 cm bs in Unit 1, although no indications of any features were observed at this depth. A small quantity of bone, but no burned clay, were recovered from Unit 1. The B horizon of the West Fork soil was encountered at a depth of approximately 200 cm bs. The last two 10-cm- levels were sterile and excavation of the unit was terminated at 220 cm bs. Unit 2 Unit 2 was positioned on the east side of Trench 2 and started at a depth of 60 cm bs. As in Unit 60 1, the first soil zone encountered was that of the dark to very dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2 to 3/2) silty clay of the 2A soil zone discussed above (Figure 11). The West Fork soil was identified at approximately 140 cm bs. Fine to large vertical krotovina were common in the upper levels of the A horizon of the West Fork, and possibly contributed to the movement of cultural material through the paleosol. Shell was not recovered in Unit 2 until 150 cm bs. An increase in the amount of shell occurred at 180 cm bs, a similar depth to the increase in shell noted in Unit 1 (see Table 7). Unlike Unit 1, however, burned clay fragments were also present at this depth, but no evidence of a cultural feature was noted. Both bone and charcoal were recovered from the soil zone above the West Fork, but only 0.1 g of charcoal and 0.1 g of bone were recovered from the West Fork itself in Unit 2. ## Page 83

Non-Artifactual Remains Forty-eight faunal specimens were recovered from Trenches 1, 2, 3, and 14 and from Units 1 and 2 at 41DL318 (see Appendix C). The size and condition of most of the elements generally precluded identification beyond the class level. One fragment of a deer (Odocoileus sp.) tooth from Trench 1 (190-200 cm bs) is the only specimen in the entire sample identifiable beyond this taxonomic level. The sample is dominated by mammal remains (n=46; 96 percent), particularly large mammals (n=29, 61 percent), probably deer, and these elements are dispersed throughout the site. The remaining four percent of the observed specimens (n=2) are represented by medium-sized birds. Unit 2 yielded 15 indeterminate mammal remains at depths of 60-70 cm bs (n=1), 100-110 cm bs (n=13), and 110-120 cm bs (n=1). The only small mammal bone in the sample was recovered from Trench 1 at 120 cm bs and may not be associated with prehistoric subsistence. Taphonomic alterations included bone exfoliation and occasional carnivore or rodent gnawing. Two specimens of large mammals from Trench 2 (200 cm bs) and Trench 14 (215 cm bs) exhibited spiral fractures which suggest, but does not necessarily indicate, human modification. In particular, one speciman with a spiral fracture also shows evidence of carnivore gnawing, which may have caused the fracture. Four species of terrestrial gastropods (n=29) and six species of freshwater bivalves (n=40) were identified at 41DL318 (see Appendix E). The species of mollusks recovered are similar to those that currently inhabit the area and suggest that the environment was similar to that of today. Specifically, the gastropods indicate riparian woodlands with adjacent open, grassy areas and a climate of hot, dry summers and relatively mild winters and the bivalves indicate a stream or river environment. Much of the bivalve assemblage was probably utilized as a prehistoric food resource, although only three specimens (one from Trench 1 at 170 to 180 cm bs, and two from Trench 14 at 145 to 65 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites 160 cm bs) showed evidence of heat treatment. These heat-treated shells roughly correspond in depth with the two possible occupation zones in Units 1 and 2. Heat treatment indicates human activity since the bivalves were processed with hot water or steam as opposed to charring, which can occur naturally and will dissolve or significantly shrivel the body inside the shell. It is also possible that some bivalves were eaten raw. The overwhelming majority of the specimens recovered from 41DL318 were subadult. This suggests that a constant collecting pressure was exerted on the local bivalve populations. No evidence was found at 41DL318 of the use of shells for tools or ornaments. Special Analyses Flotation Analyses In addition to the samples collected from Feature 3 (see above), two soil samples were collected for flotation from the West Fork soil at site 41DL318—one from Trench 1 at the top of the West Fork (Bag 201) and the other from Trench 4 at the bottom of the West Fork (Bag 202). Table 12 summarizes the contents of these flotation samples. Flotation of these samples resulted in the recovery of a small quantity of burned clay from each sample. No ecofacts or lithic artifacts were recovered from either one. Radiocarbon Dating Feature 3, in Unit 1, was the only cultural feature at 41DL318 that yielded sufficient material for radiocarbon analysis (see Appendix G). A sample of organic sediment collected from this apparent ash feature was AMS dated to 1,000 = 50 years B.P. (Beta-129633; organic sediment; 5°C = -21.4%o), with a one-sigma calibrated date of cal A.D. 1000 (1020) 1040. The sample was collected from 130-140 cm bs in Feature 1, which apparently lies at the top of the West Fork soil in this area (see Figure 10). This is

Summary Trackhoe trenches excavated at 41DL318 produced varying amounts of shell, bone, burned clay, and burned rock. Test units placed adjacent to the two. trenches most prolific in artifacts and ecofacts yielded quantities of shell, bone, charcoal, and burned clay, as well as one feature. Depth of these materials correspond between the two units, and their vertical distribution suggests two prehistoric occupation zones, one at about 130-150 cm bs and the other at about 180-200 cm bs. The upper cultural zone lies at the top of the West Fork soil (3A horizon) and is buried by recent natural levee deposits (2A horizon). The lower cultural zone lies near the base of the 3A horizon of the West Fork soil, extending slightly into the 3B horizon in Unit 2. The upper cultural zone was most pronounced in Unit 1, where it was found to be associated with Feature 3, an apparent ash deposit, possibly the remains of a hearth based on the presence of 66 Shell Bone Burned Clay Lithics Wt. (g) Wt. (g) Wt. (g) n 0.1 -- 0.2 -- 1.2 -- 2.0 -- 1d -- 2.4 - what appeared to be burned soil and charcoal in the surrounding fill. A flotation sample collected from this feature contained five pieces of microdebitage—four tertiary flakes or flake fragments and one piece of shatter, all of quartzite. A sample of organic sediment from Feature 3 yielded an AMS date of 1000 + 50 years (cal A.D. 1000 (1020) 1040). This suggests the upper cultural zone dates to the latter part of the Late Prehistoric I period, or later. Vertebrate faunal remains recovered from the upper cultural zone (ie., 130-150 cm bs) consist of only three fragments of large mammal (probably deer) from 140-150 cm bs in Trench 1, and 15 fragments of large mammal (probably deer) from 150 cm bs in Trench 2. Molluscan remains associated or possibly associated with the upper cultural zone at 41DL318 are shown in Table 13. The terrestrial gastropods (n=19) indicate a riparian bottomland environment, with open, grassy areas to partially wooded areas along streams, with a climate very similar to that of today, with hot, dry summers and relatively mild winters. Most of the bivalves (n=7, 88 percent) are deep water (pool) species and suggest that the ancestral Trinity River channel was nearby. The only evidence for human consumption comes from one heated specimen of A. plicata and one of P. dombeyanus that are assumed to be associated with the upper cultural zone (see Table 13). The preponderance of sub- adult specimens suggests that the prehistoric mussel shell population was under fairly constant pressure, implying that it was a

The presence of more adult and immature specimens in this assemblage may indicate a prehistoric mussel shell population that was under less collecting pressure from the prehistoric occupants of the area than was true later. Thus, the archeological evidence from 41DL318 suggests that the site was utilized at least twice by prehistoric hunter-gatherers residing in the Dallas area—the first time probably during the latter part of the Late Archaic period or the early part of the Late Prehistoric period, and the second time during the Late Prehistoric period (.e., sometime subsequent to A.D. 1000). No evidence was found to indicate more than a very short occupation on either occasion. Circumstantial evidence was recovered to indicate that deer and freshwater mussels were the primary faunal resources being utilized on both occasions, while no evidence of botanical resource-use was recovered. Only the later occupation yielded any evidence of lithic reduction, and this indicated only tool maintenance activities. The paucity of cultural material present suggest that this site will not yield additional information important to an understanding of prehistoric settlement- subsistence in the region. SITE 41DL319/357 Previous Investigations Site 41DL319 was also recorded by Paul Lorrain, Brenda Whorton, and Bill Young, on June 19, 1991, while working for AR Consultants on the Central Waste Water Levee Project (Skinner et al. 1991). The site was found in the southwest bank of the Trinity River, 30 m east of the former site of the Miller’s Crossing Bridge (41DL317). Site 41DL357 was recorded during a second survey of the area by S. Alan _ Skinner of AR Consultants on September 8, 1993, as part of another investigation at the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant (Skinner and Whorton 1995:16). The site was found eroding from the cutbank on the southwest side of the Trinity River. It extends 35 m to the east from the remains of Miller’s Crossing Bridge. During the current testing project, trenching revealed the deposits of mussel shell to be continuous between these two sites. Therefore, subsequent to the following discussion regarding

## Page 87

previous investigations, the sites will be combined and referred to as 41DL3 19/357. Skinner et al. (1991) identified site 41DL319 on the basis of one large bone (possibly bison), several mussel shells, a few pieces of burned rock, one secondary flake of Brazos chert, and one broken biface, possibly a Gary point preform, made of thermally altered Uvalde chert. The site stratigraphy was described as “1% m + of sandy loam over Blackland over a dark clay.” Since the cultural deposits were also described as beginning 1% m from surface, it appears likely that the “Blackland” soil is a buried A-horizon. In addition, the cultural deposits were described as being a lens not more than 20 cm thick, which suggests a short-term occupation. The period of occupation was estimated as being Late Archaic, based on the presence of a probable Gary point preform. Artifacts observed at site 41DL357 during the second survey conducted by Skinner and Whorton (1995) included burned and unburned shell, burned rock, and a chert biface, approximately 1.5 to 2 m below ground surface. As mapped by Skinner and Whorton, the site stretched east from the remnants of the Miller’s Crossing Bridge (41DL317), and seemed to overlap the area previously recorded as site 41DL319 (see above). Site 41DL319 was originally recorded in the cutbank along the southwest side of the river channel, beginning about 30 m east of the remnants of the Miller’s Crossing Bridge (41DL317), while site 41DL357 was recorded as being on a flat clay bench between the cutbank and the river, apparently stretching 35 m to the east from Miéiller’s Crossing Bridge (41DL317). Both sites 41DL319 and 41DL357 were revisited by GMI personnel in May and August of 1997. At that time, a low density scatter of shell fragments and rock was found in an eroded context along the southwest bank of the river in the area identified as 41DL319 and 41DL357. Unfortunately, the origin for this material could not be identified. In view of the apparent overlap of sites 41DL319 and 41DL357, the 69 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites possibility was acknowledged that material exposed at the latter site had been redeposited from site 41DL319. A previously unidentified low density scatter of shell fragments was found west of the remnants of the Miller’s Crossing Bridge (41DL317), but once again the field crew was unable to determine the source for this material. Although it was considered possible that these shell scatters represented the remains of a shell lens largely destroyed by erosion; it was decided that significant cultural remains could still be present in this area. Due to the presence of buried deposits, both sites 41DL319 and 41DL357 were evaluated as having the potential to yield data relevant to prehistoric research problems in Northcentral Texas, including paleoenvironmental reconstruction, culture history, settlement- subsistence systems, lithic raw material use patterns, and prehistoric technology (Cliff et al. 1998). Consequently, it was recommended that both sites 41DL319 and 41DL357 be considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP pending further investigations (Cliff et al. 1998). Current Investigations Eleven trackhoe trenches (Trench 5 through 13, 15, and 16) were excavated at this conflated site (Figure 13). As discussed in the background section, above, it was believed likely that the material identified in the cutbank of the Trinity River, and recorded as sites 41DL319 and 41DL357, might represent a single site, and excavations have proved this to be true. In order to determine the extent of the cultural deposits, 10 trackhoe trenches were excavated along the edge of the cutbank, perpendicular to the Trinity River. The trenches ranged in length from 5 to 8 m and were excavated to a minimum depth of 150 cm bs (Table 15). Trench 5 was placed at the western edge of the area originally recorded as 41DL357, directly across Honey Springs Branch from site 41DL318. This trench produced both mussel shell and bone fragments. An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas In order to determine the eastern extent of the site, Trench 10 was excavated approximately 80 m east of Trench 7, directly east of the last shell concentration previously identified in the cutbank of the Trinity River at site 41DL319. No cultural material was identified in this trench, which indicates that site 41DL319/357 did not extend this far east. This trench provided a particularly clear geological profile and was sampled every 20 cm bs from the top of the West Fork soil to the bottom of the trench at 410 cm bs. Trench 11 was placed halfway between Trench 10 and Trench 7 and, although no shell or bone was identified in this trench, an area of burned clay and associated charcoal (Feature 2) was identified in the bottom of the trench at a depth of 360 to 370 cm bs. A long bone fragment from a large mammal was collected from the cutbank of the Trinity River approximately 2 m northeast of Trench 11. Further investigation of this cutbank area revealed what appeared to be a nearly complete deer skeleton located directly above the West Fork. Trench 12 was placed halfway between Trench 11 and Trench 7 and revealed small quantities of shell and a single piece of debitage at 230 cm bs. Trench 13, placed halfway between Trench 12 and Trench 7, produced mussel shell fragments, a piece of lithic debitage, and an area of burned clay (Feature 1) at 250 to 260 cm bs. The burned clay comprised an area approximately 20 by 20 cm identified in the bottom of the trench and will be discussed in detail below. Table 16 summarizes the recovery from the trench excavations at site 41DL319/357. Six of the seven trenches excavated across the cutbank of the Trinity River produced either mussel shell, mussel shell and bone, or burned clay fragments. This indicates that sites 41DL319 and 41DL357 are in fact the same site. The only trench not to produce any cultural material was Trench 10, which was excavated at the far eastern end of the area, Based on this evidence, sites 41DL319 and 41DL357 were combined into one site (ie., 41DL319/357). Seven of the trenches excavated at the site produced mussel shell, although in 72 very small quantities. Trench 16 also contained mussel shell and burned clay; however, the material could not be quantified or collected. The most productive zone in terms of the quantity of shell recovered was from 225 to 240 cm bs in Trench 6, which produced 14.8 g of mussel shell. Bone recovered from the trenches at 41DL319/357 was limited to small fragments from Trench 5 and Trench 8. Small (1-2 mm), scattered fragments of bummed clay were identified in two of the trenches (Trench 6 and Trench 7), and concentrations of burned clay were identified in Trench 13 (Feature 1) and Trench 11 (Feature 2). The only evidence of burned rock identified at 41DL319/357 was several burned pebbles observed in the west wall of Trench 7. The only lithic artifacts recovered from the trenching activity were a single piece of shatter from Trench 12 and a flake from Trench 13, Subsequent to trenching, a total of six hand-dug units were excavated at site 41DL319/357. Units 4, 5, 7, and 8 measured .5-x-.5 m in size, while Units 3 and 6 each measured 1-x-1 m. The noncultural overburden on the west side of Trench 7 was removed and Unit 3 was initiated at 150 cm bs. The unit was placed so as to come down on the burned pebbles identified in the west wall of that trench. Unit 3 was first started as a .5-x-.5-m excavation; however, the presence of lithic material in the upper levels prompted its expansion to a 1-x-l-m unit. Due to the recovery of lithic artifacts from Unit 3, this excavation area was further expanded with the placement of Unit 6 on the south side of Unit 3. Therefore, an area measuring 1 x 2 m was excavated adjacent to Trench 7. Together, Units 3 and 6 accounted for 84 percent (n=31) of all ‘lithic material recovered from site 41DL3 19/357. Sediment and Stratigraphy The area identified as site 41DL319/357 is mapped as being frequently flooded Trinity clay (USDA, SCS 1980). The site itself is located along the elevated edge of the river bank, from near the Honey Springs Branch and the Trinity River eastward for approximately 130 m. The elevated characteristic of this area has been accentuated by the use of the area directly south of the site as a borrow pit—3 to 4 m of soil have been removed along the entire length of the site. At least a portion of 41DL319/357 appears to have remained relatively intact, despite the erosion along the river bank and the borrow pit activities to the south. Erosion has significantly affected the integrity of the site. In the short time the crew was testing the site, approximately .3 m of soil eroded away in several places along the cutbank.

## Page 92

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas Sediments revealed by trenching activities at 41DL319/357 were generally characterized by a C-2A-2C-3Abca-3Bbca-3Cca profile. Trench profiles are illustrated in Figures 14 (east to west) and 15 (north to south) and detailed in Appendix F. The C horizon consists of between 15 (Trench 12) and 57 (Trench 5) cm of recent fluvial deposits. The upper zone of this horizon consists of brown to very pale brown (10YR 5/3 to 7/4) loose, fine-grained, silty sand that exhibits fine laminations. The C horizon darkens with increasing depth to a dark grayish brown to dark brown (10YR 4/2 to 4/3). Woody roots and rootlets are abundant in the C horizon, as is orange iron staining and evidence of heavy bioturbation, particularly in the upper zone. The fluvial deposits are thickest at the west end of the site and decrease steadily toward the east. Trench 10, located beyond the eastern edge of the site, showed no evidence of a C horizon at all. A shallow C horizon was identified in both Trenches 8 and 9, which were to the south of Trench 7; however, no fluvial deposits were observed in Trench 15, which was located near the bottom of the borrow pit. It appears that borrow pit activities from the earlier 1990s have completely removed not only the fluvial deposits, but the underlying 2A and 3Abca horizons as well (see Figure 15). Modern material such as red cloth and rusty metal was observed only in Trench 5 which suggests that disturbance of this zone was limited to the western edge of the site. This disturbance is likely due to the earlier construction of a culvert between the borrow pit and Honey Springs Branch directly west of Trench 5. This channel is approximately 25 m wide from east to west, runs north-south, and has been lined with small, boulder-sized limestone fragments (see Figure 13). The C horizon boundary is abrupt across the site. The C horizon continues to a depth of 120 cm in Trench 5, although its characteristics change at approximately 60 cm from a pale brown to a dark brown (1O0YR 3/3) grading to yellowish brown (10YR 5/6) silty sand. This lower zone is also heavily bioturbated and mottled. The presence of several metal fragments indicate that 74 disturbances continue to this depth, probably due either to the movement of soil during the construction of the culvert or to modern material falling through desiccation cracks. The 2A horizon appears to represent a very late Holocene alluvium with an accumulation of humified organic matter and often exhibits evidence of disturbance. As at site 41DL318, this horizon is characterized by a very dark grayish brown to dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2 to 4/2) sandy clay with a weak to fine, blocky structure with occasional sand coats on ped faces. Small, vertical and horizontal sand-filled cracks or root casts and woody roots and rootlets are common. No evidence of modern material was identified in this zone across the site, although the 2A horizon identified in the other trenches was not observed in Trench 5 where previous disturbance has occurred. A darker subhorizon of the 2A horizon, with a higher clay content, was revealed in the middle of the site (Trenches 6 and 7) and is characterized by a very dark gray to very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/1 to 3/2) clay that extends to a depth of approximately 100 cm bs. The structure of this soil is very firm and blocky. As with the overlying A subhorizon, heavy bioturbation with abundant vertical, sand- and silt-filled cracks or root casts was observed. A piece of rusted metal was discovered in the wall of Trench 6, which indicates that disturbances occurred in this area during the historic period. Similar fragments of metal were observed along the cutbank between Trenches 5 and 6 and are presumably associated with the construction of Miller’s Crossing Bridge (site 41DL317). The bridge was located approximately 10 m west of Trench 6, as evidenced by the bridge supports still remaining in the Trinity River (see Figure 13). No other evidence of disturbance was observed in Trench 6. The overall thickness of the A horizon across the site from east to west ranges from approximately 65 cm (Trenches 6 and 13) to approximately 90 cm (Trenches 10 and 12) and generally, with the exception of the very western edge of the site (Trench 5), follows the lay of the present ground surface. ## Page 95

south cross section (see Figure 15) shows a gradual thinning of this A horizon from north to south, probably due to the borrow pit activities which removed up to approximately 3 m of soil in some areas (Trench 15). The boundary of the A horizon is abrupt and commonly bioturbated with the underlying horizon. Another C horizon was encountered in several of the trenches excavated at site 41DL319/357 (Trenches 6, 7, 10, 11, 12, and 13). This horizon consists of alternating bands of yellowish brown to very pale yellow (10YR 5/4 to 6/8) silty sand to fine-grained sand that increases in clay content and darkens gradually with increasing depth. The banding was particularly clear in Trench 10 which revealed, from the top of the C horizon down, a 25-cm-thick layer of interbedded sand and clay that terminates in clay, then 10 cm of clay that overlies 21 cm of interbedded sand and clay, then 20 cm of clay that overlies 15 cm of sand (see Appendix F). Many fine to small, and several large, krotovina were observed throughout this horizon. Small (2-4 mm in size), grayish-white calcareous concretions were common, as were white calcareous deposits lining rootlet casts. While the overlying A horizon lies primarily parallel to the present ground surface, this C horizon consists of a thin layer (10 to 20 cm) in the western portion of the site and gradually expands to a layer approximately 125 cm thick in Trench 10 located east of the site. The north-south cross section also reveals the sandy C_ horizon; however, the layer is very thin (0 to 4 cm) and was identified only in Trench 8. The boundary of the C horizon is clear, highly irregular or wavy, and appears to resemble sand ridges running parallel to the current Trinity River channel and at right angles to the trench axes across the site from east to west. These clastics, which coarsen upwards, appear to represent deposition in natural levees along the current channel margin (see Appendix F). The top of the West Fork soil, the thick, dark clay and calcium-rich cumulic soil that grades slowly into its parent material, was identified at depths that range from approximately 120 cm in Trench 5, located in the western portion of the 77 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites site, to 215 cm in Trench 10, located east of the site (see Figure 13). While the thickness of the paleosol remains relatively constant across the site, the depth of this buried soil increases significantly. In most of the trenches a West Fork A and B horizon were identified. The A (3Abca) horizon of the West Fork consists of black to very dark gray (LOYR 2/1 to 3/1) clay with a strong, blocky structure and heavy clay skins. Common characteristics include few to abundant small, calcareous concretions; fine, sand-filled krotovina, particularly in the upper zone of the horizon; and occasional decaying organic matter such as tree roots. The thickness of the A horizon ranged from 90 cm in Trench 5, at the western edge of the site, to 140 cm in Trench 7, in the middle, to 125 cm in Trench 10, east of the site. The maximum extent of the A horizon of the West Fork was not reached in Trenches 8, 12, and 13. The 3B horizon of the West Fork soil is characterized as a very dark grayish brown to dark brown (10YR 3/2 to 3/3) clay similar to the overlying A horizon but more plastic in structure. Clay coats are common on ped faces, while silt and sand coats are also apparent. An increase in the amount of calcareous concretions from the West Fork A horizon was observed. Although the B horizon of the West Fork was reached in five of the trenches excavated at site 41DL319/357, the maximum depth of this horizon was confirmed in only Trenches 7 and 10. The B horizon is 30 cm thick in Trench 7 and 40 cm thick in Trench 10. Comparison of the West Fork A and B horizons in these two trenches reveals that the West Fork soil increases slightly from 170 cm thick in Trench 7 in the west to 185 cm thick in Trench 10 in the east. While the present-day topography is relatively flat from east to west, the trench profiles indicate that the paleosol-landform decreased in elevation from west to east, nearer the main channel of the river. As at site 41DL318, the nature of the West Fork deposits identified during trenching suggests slow deposition of fine grained clastics common to the back swamp portions of an agerading flood plain, although the exact location of the river channel itself at that time is unknown.

## Page 96

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas The Cca horizon appears to represent earlier alluvial deposits of middle or early Holocene age and consists of a subsurface mineral horizon that is slightly altered parent material. It was characterized by dark brown to brown (10YR 3/3 to 5/3) clay with common calcareous concretions. This horizon was identified in Trenches 7, 9, 10, and 11, at depths that ranged from 310 to 320 cm bs (Trenches 11 and 7, respectively) to 400 cm bs (Trench 10). The Cca horizon was observed at a depth of 150 cm bs in Trench 9. However, it has already been determined that the upper soil horizons in this area have been truncated by borrow pit activity. Horizontal and Vertical Extent Based on the excavation of 11 trenches, site 41DL319/357 measures approximately 125 m east-west by 20m north-south and covers an area of approximately 2,500 m*. The north- south dimension of the site was limited by the extensive disturbance identified to the south of the site related to previous borrow pit activities. Given the number of trenches excavated at the site, the east-west dimension should be viewed as accurate. The original north-south dimension, disturbances to the south of the site and the extensive erosion along the cutbank of the river. Trenches 10 and 11 were the only trenches that did not produce artifacts or ecofacts during excavation; however, Feature 2 was identified on the floor of Trench 11 (Table 17). Therefore, the site boundary was tentatively drawn between these two trenches (see Figure 15). Mussel shell was by far the most common material recovered from the trenches and units excavated at 41DL319/357. All units and eight of the 10 trenches examined for remains produced shell (Trench 16 was excavated for sampling purposes only). Lithic artifacts were more scarce and were recovered only from Trenches 12 and 13 and Units 3, 5, and 6 (Tables 18 and 19; see Table 17): Bone was more common in the unit excavations, while burned clay was more common in the trenches. This is probably a sampling bias, as considerably more soil was moved during the excavation of the trenches, and the burned clay was very distinctive against the dark gray West Fork soil even during mechanical excavation. Based on the distribution of these four material types, various prehistoric activities appear to have occurred on the east-west ridge between Trenches 6 and 12 and Trenches 7 however, is unknown because of the and 8. Vertically, the West Fork soil was considerably more productive than the other zones identified at site 41DL319/357. The A horizon of the paleosol accounts for 99 percent (170.7 g) of all shell, 100 percent (6.4 g) of the burned clay, 91 percent (12.7 g) of all bone, and 94 percent (n=33) of all lithics recovered from the West Fork (Table 22). While recovery occurred throughout the West Fork soil, the most productive levels of shell, bone, and lithics varied across the site. Recovery increased in Units 3 and 6 at approximately 90 cm into the West Fork (230 to 240 cm bs). This increased recovery spanned approximately 30 cm. Recovery in Unit 4 was relatively evenly scattered throughout the unit, except for bone, which peaked at 20 cm into the West Fork (180 to 190 cm bs). Unit 5 exhibited a small peak in mussel shell and bone approximately 30 to 40 cm into the paleosol (230 to 250 cm bs) and another peak in shell, bone, and _lithics approximately 80 cm into the West Fork (280 to 290 cm bs). Mussel shell increased slightly in both Units 7 and 8 approximately 40 to 50 cm into the paleosol (200 and 130 cm bs, respectively). Although it is tempting to interpret these horizontal and vertical variations as representing repeated occupation of this site over a period of time, some caution must be exercised. The presence of modern refuse to depths of 250 cm bs within vertisolic cracks and the recovery of an introduced species, that of nutria, within Trench 13 suggest that the vertical distributions are skewed at best. Distributions of materials throughout the test units indicate three to four vertical concentrations of material (see Tables 18 and 19). The uppermost concentrations begin 0 to 20 cm into the West Fork soil of Units 3, 4, 5, and 8 and extend an additional 20 cm. These artifact/ecofact concentrations for Units 4 and 5, the easternmost test units, indicate greater below surface depths (170 to 190 cm bs and 230 to 250 cm bs, respectively) because the top of the West Fork soil appears increasingly deeper toward the east side of the site. A possible prior concentration may be indicated by noticeable West Fork soil in Units 3 (190 to 210 cm bs) and 7 (200-210 cm bs). Shell accumulations are sparser at this level than in other possible amounts of shell 40 to 60 cm into the occupation levels in Unit 3. However, shell amounts in the

Units 3 and 6 Unit 3 (1-x-1 m) was positioned on the west side of Trench 7 so as to come down on top of several limestone pebbles identified in the west wall of Trench 7 at 250 cm bs (see Figure 13). The unit was initiated at 160 cm bs at the top of the West Fork soil. The recovery of lithic artifacts from the 220 to 230 cm bs level of Unit 3, prompted the placement of Unit 6 (1-x-1 m) on its south side. The profile for the two units is provided in Figure 16. The apparent complexity of the profile appears to be primarily the result 82 of the clay of the West Fork drying at varying speeds. The extreme heat during the period of field work coupled with the increased size of these units, which delayed their completion, caused the desiccation cracks that were already present to increase in size and depth. Therefore, during periods of rain, of which there were several during the investigations, the depth to which the rain reached depended on the presence of these cracks. This resulted in what appeared to be “pits” or subzones within the West Fork soil that were either darker or more friable because these soils were moister than the surrounding soil (see Figure 16). Variations in the West Fork were limited to occasional yellowish brown (10YR 5/6) clay mottles in zone Abca-II and a gradual lightening of the soil color as depth increased. The quantity of calcareous concretions also increased below approximately 240 cm bs. The desiccation cracks so common to this area were observed throughout the excavations, as evidenced by yellowish brown (10YR 5/8) sand-filled cracks and krotovina. A small piece of foil and a fragment of blue cloth were observed in the soil matrix at a depth of 210 to 220 cm bs in Unit 3. Because no evidence of disturbance beyond the presence of desiccation cracks was observed, the presence of modern material at this depth is attributed to these features. The Trinity River

## Page 102

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas and its banks are literally lined with similar material and the trenches excavated at site 41DL319/357 were exposed for over a month. Because the primary reason for the excavation of Unit 6 was to sample the most productive levels, and given the amount of time screening required, the upper levels of Unit 6 were excavated without being screened. Screening of the excavated matrix began at a depth of 210 cm bs, the level where bone was first recovered from Unit 3. Mussel shell was relatively sparse in both units until the 240 cm bs level where there was a significant increase in the amount of shell recovered from Unit 6. Very little shell was recovered below 260 cm bs. Bone and lithic artifacts were collected from similar depths in both units—between 210 and 280 cm bs in Unit 3 and between 230 and 260 cm bs in Unit 6. Thirty-one lithic artifacts were recovered from Units 3 and 6 and are summarized in Table 23. Of the 34 lithic artifacts recovered from Units 3 and 6, two are projectile points—one, was broken into three fragments during excavation (Bag 121, No. 5) and the other, an indeterminate point type, consists of only the tip (Bag 121, No. 1; see Appendix A). Both tools were recovered from Unit 3 from the shallowest level that produced lithic material (220 to 230 cm bs), which is approximately 120 cm into the West Fork soil. The quantity of lithic artifacts gradually declined between 220 and 280; the last levels excavated (280 to 290 cm bs in Unit 3 and 270 to 280 cm bs in Unit 6) yielded no artifacts. Burned clay fragments were recovered in small amounts from Unit 6 only. A soil sample (Beta- 129629) removed from Unit 3 at a depth of 220- 240 cm bs was submitted for radiocarbon dating (see Radiocarbon Dating discussion below). The sample produced a date of 2,380 + 70 B.P. (see Appendix G), which corresponds with the Late Archaic period in the upper Trinity River basin. During the excavation of Unit 3 and following a heavy rain, a portion of the unit adjacent to Trench 7 slumped into the bottom of the trench. Unfortunately this portion contained the three limestone pebbles identified in the wall of the trench. However, no evidence of a cultural feature was identified in the remaining matrix at that level or throughout the excavation of either Unit 3 or Unit 6. Excavations were terminated at 290 cm bs and 280 cm bs, respectively, when sterile levels were encountered. ## Page 103

Unit 4 Unit 4 (1-x-1 m) was positioned on the east side of Trench 13 in order to come down adjacent to Feature 1, which was identified in the bottom of the trench (see Figure 13). It was initiated at a depth of 170 cm bs, at the very top of the West Fork. It was decided that a 1-x-l-m unit was more likely than a .5-x-.5-m unit to reveal any remains of Feature 1, should they be present. The A horizon of the West Fork was the only soil zone encountered during the excavation of Unit 4, although slight variations in the soil were observed. Yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) sand- filled krotovina were common in the West Fork and decreased as depth increased, as did the occurrence of calcareous concretions. The same phenomenon that was noted in Units 3 and 6 was also observed during the excavation of Unit 4—during the length of time the unit was excavated, extreme heat and heavy rains enlarged and exaggerated desiccation cracks. Therefore the unit profile appears to illustrate “pits” in the upper West Fork which are actually areas more saturated with moisture than the surrounding soil (Figure 17). No lithic artifacts were recovered, even though Trench 13 did produce a piece of lithic debitage. Recovery from Unit 4 consisted of small quantities of shell and bone to a depth of 260 cm bs, and a very small amount of burned clay between 230 and 250 cm bs. Aside from the 12 small fragments that together weighed 0.2 g, no evidence of the burned clay of Feature 1 (245 to 255 cm bs) was observed during the excavation of the unit. Excavation was terminated at a depth of 260 cm bs. Unit 5 Unit 5 (.5-x-.5 m) was positioned on the east side of Trench 12, one of two trenches (Trenches 12 and 13) to produce a single piece of debitage during excavation (see Figure 13). The unit was initiated at 180 cm bs, but excavations did not expose the top of the West Fork until approximately 220 cm bs (Figure 18). As with the other units excavated at 41DL319/357, Unit 85 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites 5 yielded small mussel shell fragments throughout its excavation. The most productive levels in terms of shell were between 230 and 250 cm bs, approximately 20 to 40 cm into the West Fork. An increase in the quantity of small calcareous concretions was also noted in these same levels (between 233 and 243 cm bs). The 280 to 290 cm bs level was the only 10-cm level to produce shell, bone, burned clay, a single piece of debitage, and a single burned rock. Unit 5 yielded more burned clay than any other unit excavated at 41DL319/357, although no concentration of burned clay was observed. Unit 7 Unit 7 (.5-x-.5 m) was positioned adjacent to Trench 6, the only trench from which charcoal was collected during excavation. The placement of Unit 6 was intended to sample the west side of the site; however, disturbance was moderate in this area of the site and excavation of units was kept to a minimum (see Figure 13). The cleared area in which Unit 7 was placed was approximately 25 to 35 cm above the West Fork. Therefore, the upper 30 cm of the unit, from 130 to 160 cm bs, were excavated without being screened. Screening of the soil matrix excavated from Unit 7 began at 160 to 170 cm bs, at the top of the West Fork soil (Figure 19). The profile for Unit 7 is very typical for this site—the black to very dark gray clay of the upper West Fork gradually increased in lightness as well as in the amount of calcareous concretions as- depth increased. Recovery from Unit 7 was limited to mussel shell and a small fragment of bone between 180 and 230 cm bs, approximately 30 to 70 cm into the West Fork. Feature 4 was identified adjacent to the southeast corner of the unit, in the west wall of Trench 6. The burned soil of this feature was first observed at 180 cm bs and extended to 220 cm bs. The feature, discussed in detail below, was interpreted as a burned root and not a feature resulting from cultural activity. Excavation of Unit 7 was terminated at 250 cm bs after two, 10-cm levels of sterile soil were removed.

## Page 107

Unit 8 Unit 8 was excavated adjacent to Trench 8, south of the initial series of trenches (see Figure 13). It was started at 100 cm bs, which was approximately 20 cm into the West Fork soil (Figure 20). Recovery from the unit consisted of a small quantity of mussel shell only. This supported the conclusion that the focus of activity at 41DL319/357 was along the elevated ridge adjacent to the cut bank. Excavation was terminated at 150 cm bs after a single sterile 10- cm level was removed. Cultural Features Three features were observed at 41DL319/357— all three of which consisted of burned clay concentrations. Feature 1 Feature 1 was encountered during the mechanical excavation of Trench 13 at 245 cm bs. The trackhoe bucket scraped an unknown portion of the feature, and its original size is not known. However, excavation of the trench was halted immediately and the feature was documented, bisected, and sampled in order to avoid any further destruction which may have resulted if the feature were left open to the elements. A plan view of the bottom of Trench 13 and Feature 1 is provided in Figure 21, while a plan view of the bisected feature and its profile are presented in Figure 22. The feature was located near the eastern edge of the trench and consisted of several chunks of solid, reddish brown (2.5YR 4/4) burned clay, that together measured approximately 4 cm east to west by 8 cm north to south. These chunks were located near the center of a less concentrated area of orange burned clay scattered throughout a very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay that measured approximately 18 cm north to south by 20 cm east to west. The feature was identified 89 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites approximately 80 cm into the West Fork soil. Drying and subsequent cracking of the feature, due to weather conditions, made bisecting the chunks of clay difficult. The west half of the feature was collected for flotation and the remaining profile of the feature was documented. The less concentrated mixture of very dark gray clay and burned clay fragments extended to a depth of approximately 255 cm bs, while the solid chunks of burned clay extended only to approximately 247 cm bs. The entire feature exhibited a roughly concave shape in profile. No lithic artifacts or charcoal were found associated with the feature during its excavation. After documentation, the east half of the feature was also collected for flotation. In addition, a sample of the burned soil (Beta- 129632) was collected from Feature 1 and produced a radiocarbon date of 2,750 + 60 B.P. (see Radiocarbon Dating discussion below). Unit 4 (1-x-1 m) was positioned in the area cleared by the trackhoe on the east side of Trench 13 with the intent of coming down adjacent to Feature 1 (see Figure 22). Excavation of this unit to 260 cm bs revealed no evidence of Feature 1, and no _ other concentrations of burned clay were identified. Flotation of the samples collected from Feature 1, six liters of soil from the west half of the feature, and four liters from the east half, revealed burned clay fragments and a small amount of mussel shell and bone (Table 24). The burned clay fragments ranged in size from 2 to 34 mm and weighed a total of 12 g. The mussel shell consisted of only very fragmented pieces and weighed 6 g. The single bone fragment recovered from the flotation samples was identified as mammalian and weighed .1 g. No other ecofacts or artifacts were identified in the flotation material. Feature 1 appears to represent the remains of intense burning activity; whether this activity was cultural or natural is not clear. No lithic artifacts were found in association with the feature. Furthermore, the density of shell and bone recovered from the feature is no higher

(g) Wt. (g) Wt. (g) Feature | Trench 13, 245-265 cm bs 213 & 214 10 0.6 0.1 12.0 than that for the surrounding soil (see Unit 4 discussion). The shape of Feature 1, however, did not resemble the remains of a burned root, as Feature 4 so clearly did (see below). Rather, it bore a strong resemblance to Feature 2 in terms of size, shape, and predominance of burned clay. Feature 2, which was identified in the bottom of Trench 11, had cultural associations (see below). Therefore, it is quite possible that Feature 1 is also of cultural origin. Feature 2 Feature 2 was also identified during trenching activities. The excavation of Trench 11 revealed the burned clay feature at a depth 370 cm bs, approximately 60 cm below the surface of the West Fork soil. Trenching immediately ceased and the feature was covered with black plastic until it could be properly documented. As with other features identified during trenching activities, it is not known how much of the feature was scraped away by the bucket. The remains of the feature consisted of two chunks of dusky red (2.5YR 3/2) burned clay surrounded by less concentrated fragments of burned clay scattered throughout a dark grayish brown (1OYR 4/2) clay (Figure 23). The surrounding soil was the same dark grayish brown clay but with no burned clay fragments. The chunks of burned clay in the middle of the feature measured approximately 7 by 2 cm and 3 by 2cm, and extended approximately 25 cm deep (Figure 24; see Figure 23). The overall feature measured approximately 18 cm east to west and 20 cm north to south at its top and gradually decreased in size with increased depth (see Figure 24). At 380 cm bs, the scatter of burned clay fragments measured approximately 5 by Scm. A small fragment of charcoal (Beta- 93 129634) was recovered from the southern edge of the feature. Radiocarbon analysis resulted in a date of 2,140 + 60 B.P. (see Radiocarbon Dating discussion below). Another small piece of charcoal was found approximately 3 cm north of the feature at a depth of approximately 380 cm bs. Single mussel shell fragments were observed northeast of the feature at 372 and 380 cm bs. No lithic artifacts or bone fragments were observed during the excavation of the feature. Feature 2 was bisected to a depth of 385 cm bs, and the northern half of the feature was collected for flotation. The profile of Feature 2 revealed an approximately 7-cm wide shaft of very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) clay that narrowed at the base of the feature. While small fragments of burned clay were scattered throughout this darker clay, they were not as dense as in the 10YR 4/2 clay and burned clay mixture that comprised most of the feature. Following documentation, the southern half of Feature 2 was also collected for flotation. As with Feature 1, burned clay fragments and small amounts of mussel shell, bone, and charcoal were recovered from flotation of samples from Feature 2 (Table 25). The burned clay fragments ranged in size from 2 to 11 mm and weighed a total of 7.8 gs. Much of the burned clay that comprised the feature appears to have disintegrated during the flotation process. The shell totaled .1 g and consisted primarily of the two pieces of shell observed during excavation of the feature. The bone, which weighed 0.02 g was too small for identification. One chert tertiary flake was recovered at 370 to 385 cm bs. It measured 7 x 3 x .7 mm and did not demonstrate evidence of thermal treatment.

## Page 116

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas 3/2) loamy clay with small charcoal flecks at its center, surrounded by a layer of burned or oxidized clay. The surrounding matrix was the very dark gray clay typical of the West Fork soil. The top of the feature was identified approximately 70 cm below the top of the West Fork and extended into the B horizon of the paleosol. The entire fill from the feature was collected for flotation; however, no mussel shell or lithic artifacts were recovered. Indeed, no indications that the feature resulted from a cultural activity were observed. The circular cross section, small size, and _ tapering characteristic strongly suggest that Feature 4 was a burned root. Artifactual Remains Thirty-nine lithic artifacts were collected from 41DL319/357 (Table 26; see Appendix A). All but two of these—a heat-treated chert side scraper (Bag 131, No. 1) and a Gary point of Ogallala quartzite (Bag 130, No. 1)—were collected from the subsurface excavations (Figure 27). A chert tertiary flake was recovered from a flotation sample from Feature 2 at 370 to 385 cm bs; nonetheless recovery of lithic artifacts was primarily restricted to Units 3, 5, and 6. Of these, Unit 3 yielded most of the artifacts (see Tables 18 and 19), including two tools—the tip of a point of indeterminate type (Bag 121, No.1) and three fragments which constituted a projectile point, possibly Kent (Bag 121, No. 5). Both points were made from Ogallala quartzite. The majority of the lithic material collected from 41DL319/357 consisted of flakes and flake fragments. Tools and primary and secondary flakes were generally restricted to the upper two levels (220 to 240 cm bs) of the lithic deposits, which includes the level that contained the greatest concentration of lithic materials (230 to 240 cm bs). Materials in the lower levels tended to be smaller flakes that likely worked their way deeper into the ground. With the exception of retouched piece of andesite shatter and a tertiary flake from a flotation sample from Unit 5 (190 to 200 cm bs) made from Ogallala quartzite, all lithic flakes were chert. One chert flake had been burned; otherwise, none of the flakes evidenced heat treatment. Table 26 Lithic Artifacts Recovered from Site 41DL319/357 Bifacial Indet. ## Page 117

a Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites Figure 27. Projectile points collected from site 41DL319/357: (a) Gary point; and (b) possible Kent point (Scale 1:1). Non-Artifactual Remains One hundred twelve faunal specimens were recovered from this site (see Appendix C). However, 55 of these consisted of an articulated deer skeleton from Unit 9 at 40-50 cm bs. Its depth and state of articulation indicate that it is not related to prehistoric subsistence. Most faunal remains were lightly dispersed throughout the ground surface, trackhoe trenches, and test units, although Unit 4 produced the greatest amount (n=25). Small quantities (< .1 g) of bone were recovered from flotation samples from Test Unit 7 and Features 1, 2, and 3. Except for a mammalian bone fragment, none of these specimens could be identified beyond the subphylum level. All recovered material that extended into the West Fork soil (n=57) was mammalian. The majority (45.6 percent, n=26) was identified as mammal or large mammal. Pocket gopher, nutria, canid, deer, and medium artiodactyl remains were recovered in small amounts. Except for the dog and rodent remains, most others are consistent for deer. Seven specimens from this site indicate burning and one 99 demonstrates a spiral fracture associated with carnivore gnawing. Five species of terrestrial gastropods (n=46) and eight species of freshwater bivalves (n=57) were identified at 41DL319/357 (see Appendix E). The species of mollusks recovered are similar to those that currently inhabit the area and suggest that the environment was similar to that of the present. Specifically, the gastropods indicate riparian woodlands with adjacent open, grassy areas and a climate of hot, dry summers and relatively mild winters and the bivalves indicate a stream or river environment. One charred specimen was recovered from Unit 6 at 250 to 260 cm bs. However, no heat-treated or charred specimens were recovered from the features or any of the other test units. Flood deposition may best explain the shell accumulations at this site, although this possibility is discordant with the interpretation of the bivalve assemblages from the other sites in this project area. Furthermore, the charred specimen was recovered from a unit and level that produced definite cultural materials. No evidence was found for the use of shells for tools or ornaments.

## Page 118

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas In addition, one fish otolith was recovered from Unit 3 at 270 to 280 cm bs and submitted to Mr. William McClure for analysis (see Appendix D). It was identified as coming from a freshwater drum (Aplodinotus grunniens) that measured approximately 60 cm in length. The bone could have arrived at the site via flood water transport, raptor transport, or human activity. The mussel shells recovered from the site probably aided in the otolith’s preservation by increasing or maintaining the soil alkalinity of the surrounding soil. Therefore, it is probable that the bone was deposited at roughly the same time as the shells. Special Analyses Flotation Analyses In addition to the samples collected from Features 1, 2, and 4, two flotation samples each were collected from Unit 3 and Unit 5. For Unit 3, samples were collected from the levels that produced the most lithic artifacts—one from Table 27 between 220 and 240 cm bs and the other from between 240 and 260 cm bs. Flotation samples were also taken from the upper level of Unit 5 (190 to 200 cm bs) and from the one level of the unit that produced a lithic artifact (280 to 290 cm bs). These samples yielded a small amount of bummed clay in the form of small granules. No ecofacts were present in either unit. However, the upper sample from Unit 5 produced a tertiary flake of Ogallala quartzite (Table 27). It was expected that, if micro-flakes were present at any of the flood plain sites tested during this project, they would appear in samples from site 41DL319/357, since this was the only site to produce lithics during unit excavation. However, only two flakes (including Feature 2) were recovered from floated samples. The general absence of lithic material recovered using this method at site 41DL319/357 further supports the conclusion that the sparse nature of lithic material at flood plain sites along the Trinity is more a reflection of the distribution of the material itself, rather than the sampling strategies employed. ## Page 119

Radiocarbon Dating ) Four soil samples and one charcoal sample were submitted for radiocarbon analysis (see Appendix G). Soil samples from the top (Beta- 12935) and bottom (Beta-129636) of the West Fork soil suggest a range of 3,050 + 60 to 1,420 + 60 B.P. (1,510 to 1,750 years) for the paleosol. Samples taken at various depths of the West Fork soil fall within this range. Soil taken from Unit 3 at 220 to 240 cm bs (Beta-129629), at the highest concentration of cultural material at the site, yielded a date of 2,380 + 70 B.P. Burned clay soil from Feature 1 (Beta-129632), 245 to 255 cm bs dated to 2,750 + 60 B.P., and charcoal from Feature 2 (Beta-129634) at 370 to 385 cm bs produced a radiocarbon date of 2,140 + 60 B.P. All dates produced from these samples are consistent with the Late Archaic period in the upper Trinity River basin. A point recovered from Unit 3, possibly a Kent point, is also suggestive of Archaic period occupation, as is a Gary point recovered from the site’s surface, although its association is more questionable. Summary Trackhoe trenches and hand excavated test units at site 41DL319/357 revealed concentrations of materials that consisted of mussel shell, animal bone, burned clay, and a few lithic artifacts, including two dart points. In addition, three burned clay features were discovered. One was determined to have been a burned tree root. Another was associated with a lithic flake and, therefore, indicates a possible cultural origin. The remaining feature is more similar in appearance to the latter feature, although it yielded no definitive cultural remains. Interpretation of the site deposits is severely hindered by the significant bioturbation represented by the presence of modern refuse at depths of 250 cm. bs. Although the limited ‘diagnostic artifacts, two dart points, are in concordance with the Late Archaic period as defined by the radiocarbon dates, the potential for significant bioturbation raises serious questions concerning the validity of the humate 101 Chapter 5; Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites dates and the context of the charcoal dates. Radiocarbon dates of the West Fork soil profile at site 41DL319/357 indicate a range of 3,050 + 60 to 1,420 + 60 B.P. (1,510 to 1,750 years) for the paleosol, and samples from levels of probable human occupation range from 2,750 + 60 B.P. to 2,140 + 60 B.P. The significance of these data in light of previous investigations within the upper Trinity River basin will be discussed in Chapter 6. Faunal analysis indicates the presence of fresh- water drum, deer, and mammalian bone which, at best, could only be identified to the Order level. All faunal vertebrate evidence is consistent for exploitation of environmentally available resources for subsistence; however, no definite indication of human utilization is evident. Similarly, unambiguous evidence of human activity was not observed in the recovered bivalve assemblage, and it is likely that most of the shells were flood deposited. However, the one recovered charred shell specimen did coincide with a level that produced definite cultural material. Therefore, it is possible that the site inhabitants consumed raw as well as cooked clams. Materials of unequivocal cultural origin are sparse, and cutbank erosion, bioturbation, and borrow pit activities from the early 1990s have compromised the site’s integrity. Therefore, it is recommended that site 41DL319/357 be considered ineligible for inclusion in the NRHP. SITE 41DL337 Previous Investigations Site 41DL337 was recorded by S. Alan Skinner of AR Consultants on September 8, 1992, during construction of an effluent outflow channel on the northeast side of the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant (Skinner and Whorton 1995:15). The site was identified along the cutbank on the west side of the outflow channel that was under construction in 1991 and south of the small east-

## Page 120

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas west drainage channel that empties into the outflow channel. The site is reported to have contained bison and deer bone, burned mussel shells, and one chert flake. The deposit appeared to be relatively thin, approximately 10 cm in depth, and at a depth of approximately 3 m below ground surface. The site was identified when a cluster of mussel shells approximately 2m square was “exposed and removed” by a machine scraper during construction of the outflow channel. The cluster was the thickness of a single layer of mussel shells and exhibited evidence of burning. No other. artifacts were noted in association with the shell lens. After monitoring of the site for over a year, Skinner determined that the artifacts at site 41DL337 were not in situ, but rather disturbed by channel or earlier construction. Although no diagnostic artifacts were identified, occupation was placed in the Late Prehistoric period. This conclusion was probably based on the presence of faunal material identified as bison bone. The site area was revisited by GMI personnel on May 14, 1997, but the water level at that time was at ground surface, which precluded any investigation of the site. The site was revisited again on August 5, 1997, at which time the water level was an estimated 2.5 m below ground surface. The area was heavily overgrown by weeds, and observation of a large area of the bank failed to locate mussel shell or cultural material. Given the proximity of sites 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356, and the description of all sites as being at a depth of approximately 3 m below ground surface, it was believed possible that these four sites represent either multiple activity areas or components of a single site impacted by the construction of the effluent outflow channel. Excavations elsewhere in the Trinity River basin suggested that widespread, low density, components may be present in the area. Site 41DL337, therefore, 102 was felt to have potential to yield data relevant to prehistoric research issues in Northcentral Texas, including paleoenvironmental reconstruction, culture history, settlement-subsistence systems, lithic raw material use patterns, and prehistoric technology. Consequently, it was recommended that the site be considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP pending further investigations (Cliff et al. 1998). Current Investigations Phase 2 testing of sites 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356 was initiated with the excavation of a series of 25 trackhoe trenches (Figure 28). As at sites 41DL318 and 41DL319/357, this strategy was employed in order to determine the horizontal and vertical extent of the cultural deposits at the four sites, as well as to gather enough data to generate a reliable model of buried topographic landforms and to identify the paleosol or buried surface associated with the cultural deposits identified in the bank of the effluent outflow channel. The area identified as site 41DL337 by Skinner is located on a slightly elevated ridge that is approximately 2 m above the existing water level and parallels the southeast-northwest-trending outflow channel. The excavation of a series of 10 trackhoe trenches numbered Trenches 21 through 28, 35, and 41 (Table 28; see Figure 28) were required to determine the horizontal and vertical extent of the cultural deposits. All trenches were positioned perpendicular to the water in a row that extended from the east bank of the north-south outflow channel around the northwest-trending point to the southeast. Trench 35 was excavated to the south of the first row of trenches in order to determine the southern edge of the site as well as to identify the extent of the settling pond disturbances encountered at site 41DL356 to the south. The trenches were placed 10 to 30 m apart and ranged in length from 5 to 6.5 m.
