Generally, the mussel shell recovered from all four units excavated at 41DL337 was sparse and fragmented. Likewise, the burned clay observed consisted of small (1- 2mm in size), dispersed fragments in Units 1 and 2. Sediment and Stratigraphy The area identified as site 41DL337 is mapped as consisting of frequently flooded Trinity clay and is directly northeast of an area identified as disturbed due to the removal of the limestone or shale bedrock (USDA, SCS 1980). The area directly south of 41DL337 was used as a settling pond in the early days of the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant and was later filled in and planted with saplings. At least a portion of the ridge occupied by 41DL337 appears relatively intact, despite the disturbance to the south of the site and the excavation of the outflow channel directly north of the site. The sediments at 41DL337 revealed in the trench excavations were characterized by a C-AC- 2Abca-2Bca profile (Figures 29 and 30; see Appendix F). The C horizon consists of 20 to Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites Table 29 Summary of Recovery from Trackhoe Trenches Excavated at Site 41DL337 Depth of Material (cm bs) 105 Bone Burned Clay 50 cm of recent fluvial deposits. This horizon consists of finely bedded and laminated yellowish brown (10YR 5/4), fine grained sand and dark grayish brown to brown (10YR 4/2 to 4/3) clayey silts. The fluvial deposits are slightly thicker on the eastern side of the site that drops approximately 1 m from the point at the northwestern edge of the site. The boundary between the C horizon and the next soil horizon is abrupt and generally regular across the site, although Trench 27 exhibited an irregular boundary. The AC horizon represents a very late Holocene soil development that consists of a mineral horizon: of parent material (C) with an accumulation of humified organic matter (A). The mineral portion of the horizon is dominant. This horizon is characterized by a dark grayish brown (2.5Y 4/2; 10OYR 4/2) clay with brown mottling that exhibits a weak blocky structure, heavy clay skins on ped faces, and common calcareous deposits along rootlet casts. Small calcareous concretions to 4 mm in size were observed throughout the horizon, as were small to large vertical, sand-filled pockets, probably representing sand falling through dehydration cracks or root cavities. The boundary of this AC horizon is gradual to diffuse. The AC horizon is

## Page 126

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas discontinuous across the site. It ranges from 120cm thick in Trench 23, near the northwestern end of the site, to nonexistent in Trenches 27 and 28 at the southeastern end of the site. While some of this variability may be explained by depositional factors, the fact that the AC horizon is absent in Trenches 27 and 28, both of which uncovered the settling pond, suggests the differences in the thickness of this horizon are due more to modern activities associated with the wastewater treatment plant. The AC horizon itself exhibits evidence of disturbance in several trenches. The upper 20 cm of the AC horizon in Trench 21 and the upper 50 cm in Trenches 25 and 26 appear heavily mottled and exhibit a platy, compressed appearance suggestive of organic disturbance fill. Occasional limestone fragments and decaying organic matter (roots, tree trunks) were observed. Due to the extensive disturbance identified in the area of 41DL337, it is unclear exactly how much of this clay-rich horizon is in situ. Furthermore, the sandy silty nature of the fluvial deposits overlying the AC _ horizon suggests that currents during periods of flooding are of sufficient energy to erode pre-existing deposits (see Appendix F). The disturbance noted in the upper levels of the AC horizon does indicate, however, that the disturbances associated with construction of the settling pond just south of site 41DL377 occurred before the accumulation of the interbedded fluvial deposits (C horizon) and toward the end of the accumulation of the clay-rich AC horizon. The next soil horizon identified in the trench excavations was that of the West Fork soil, a thick, dark clay and calcium-rich cumulic soil that grades slowly into its parent material (see Appendix F). The West Fork soil is divided into an A and a B horizon. While the maximum depth of the West Fork A horizon (Abca or Ab) was reached in several trenches excavated at site 41DL337, the West Fork B horizon (B or Bca) continued below the maximum depth of the trenches in which it was identified. The A horizon of the West Fork consists of very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay with a strong, blocky structure. Common characteristics include calcareous deposits along rootlet casts, vertical 108 sand-filled pockets, particularly in the upper zone of the horizon, and fewer calcareous concretions than in the overlying AC horizon. The depth of the upper boundary of the West Fork A horizon varies across the site, from approximately 85 cm bs at the northwest end of the site (Trench 41) to 140 cm bs in the middle of the site (Trench 25), to 43 cm bs on the southeast end of the site (Trenches 27 and 28). The maximum depth of the West Fork A horizon was not encountered on the northwest end of the site, while the maximum depth at the southeast side of the site varied from 245 to 300 cm bs. The variation in thickness of the A horizon of the West Fork ranged from 115 cm in Trench 24 to at least 230 cm thick in Trench 41 (see Figure 29). The B horizon of the West Fork is characterized as a dark gray brown to brown (10YR 4/1 to 10YR 4/3) clay with coarse to fine blocky structure. Silt coats are common on ped faces, while there is an increase in the amount of calcareous concretions from the West Fork A horizon. As stated above, the West Fork B horizon was reached in only four (Trenches 24, 25, 26, and 27) of the 10 trenches excavated at 41DL337. This is partially due to modern disturbances in the western (Trenches 21 and 22) and eastern (Trench 28) edges of the site where trench excavation was terminated at the first evidence of the settling pond. Although the West Fork soil appeared intact in Trenches 41 and 23, the B horizon was not encountered in these trenches because of the increased depth of the A horizon. The north-south cross section of site 41DL337 in Figure 30 illustrates the extent and proximity of the disturbance that resulted from the creation of the settling pond south of the site. Trench 35 was excavated only 5 m south of Trench 25 and revealed fill that consisted of limestone fragments, abundant decaying organic matter, and what appeared to be a rock cap, directly below the recent alluvium. The clay-rich AC horizon. and the West Fork soil encountered in the trenches directly to the north had been completely removed.

## Page 127

Horizontal and Vertical Extent Based on the excavation of eight trenches, four of which produced mussel shell (Trenches 24, 26, 27, and 41), site 41DL337 measures approximately 53 m northwest-southeast by 9 m northeast-southwest, and covers an area of approximately 498 m*. As with the other sites investigated for this project, the size of the site was limited by the erosion caused by the nearby outflow channel, as well as the disturbances related to the settling pond directly south of the site. Only four of the eight trenches excavated at 41DL337 produced mussel shell remains, and these were in relatively sparse amounts. No other ecofacts, burned clay, or lithic artifacts were recovered from the trench excavations. Table 30 summarizes the recovery from the four trenches. Although the overburden was cleared adjacent to Trench 27, time constraints and the disturbance observed at the south end of Trench 27 precluded unit excavation there, and the trench was backfilled. Recovery in the units was very sparse. Small fragments of mussel shell were found in each of the four units, while small fragments of burned clay were observed in only Units 1 and 2 (Table 31). Unit 2 was the most productive of the four 109 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites units, and accounted for 52 percent (1.1 g) of the mussel shell and 98 percent (34 g) of the burned clay recovered from the units. Mussel shell density was extremely low (Table 32). The highest density of shell occurred in Units 13 and 14, primarily because shell was found in only one level in each of the two units. The density of burned clay was higher for Unit 2, although the clay was not found in a concentrated form that would suggest a cultural feature. Given the general low recovery for the site as a whole, the density figures given below are relatively ineffectual for intrasite comparison and analysis. All of the shell and burned clay yielded by the units were recovered from the upper portion of the A horizon of the West Fork soil. Although sparse, shell fragments from Unit 2 at 140 to 150 cm bs indicate heat treatment and, therefore, human activity (see Non-Artifactual Remains discussion). ## Page 130

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas depth with the lighter silty clay dominating at the bottom of the unit. Except for several burned clay fragments in the upper soil zone, few inclusions were noted. Calcareous inclusions were more common in the lower zone. Units 1 and 2 were terminated at 200 cm bs after sterile levels were encountered in both units. Recovery consisted of 1.1 g of mussel shell between 100 and 190 cm bs and two small (6 and 2 mm) burned clay fragments that were recovered between 160 and 180 cm bs. Fragments of heat treated shell were recovered from 140 to 150 cm bs in Unit 2. No concentrations of bummed clay or shell were observed during the excavation of Units 1 and 2, and no features were identified. Unit 13 Unit 13 measured .5-x-.5 m and was excavated adjacent to Trench 24. The datum was placed at 130 cm bs and Unit 13 started with the 140 to 150 cm bs level. The West Fork soil was the only soil zone revealed during the excavations. The soil profile for Unit 13 resembles that of Units 1 and 2, although the lighter very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) silty clay was encountered at a greater depth—approximately 220 cm bs instead of 142 to 177 cm bs (Figure 32). Occasional dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2), clay-filled krotovina were observed in the upper, very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay. Recovery consisted only of 0.2 g (n=3) of mussel shell between 220 and 230 cm bs. No burned clay or other remains were observed. Excavation of the unit was discontinued at 230 cm bs for two reasons—excavation any deeper would have required expansion of the unit, and trenching activities had revealed that the majority of the cultural remains were located within the upper, darker zone of the West Fork which had been penetrated in Unit 13 without recovery. Unit 14 Unit 14 measured .5-x-.5 m and was excavated adjacent to Trench 26. The datum for this trench was established at a depth of 150 cm bs and the 112 unit was started at 150 to 160 cm bs. The profile for Unit 14 resembled that of Unit 13, except that the slightly lighter silty clay of the West Fork was encountered 10cm lower (approximately 230 cm bs; Figure 33). Recovery consisted of .7 g (n=3) of mussel shell recovered from 220 to 230 cm bs, the same level in which was found the only shell from Unit 13. Excavation of the unit was terminated at 250 cm bs after two sterile levels were encountered. No burned clay or other ecofacts were observed. Non-Artifactual Remains Three species of terrestrial gastropods (n=11) and nine species of fresh-water bivalves (n=16) were identified at this site (see Appendix E). The species of mollusks recovered are similar to those that currently inhabit the area and suggest that the environment was similar to that of the present. Specifically, the gastropods indicate riparian woodlands with adjacent open, grassy areas and a climate of hot, dry summers and relatively mild winters and the bivalves indicate a stream or river environment. Bivalve shells were sparse at site 41DL337 and all were fragmented. Nonetheless, several of the specimens recovered exhibited heat treatment. This indicates human activity because the bivalves were processed with hot water or steam rather than by charring, which can occur naturally and will dissolve or significantly shrivel the body inside the shell. The overwhelming majority of the specimens were immature. This suggests a constant collecting pressure was exerted on the local bivalve populations. No evidence was found for the use of shells for tools or ornaments. Summary The testing of site 41DL337 identified a scattered deposit of fragmented mussel shell and small burned clay granules in the upper levels of the West Fork soil. No artifacts or features were identified at the site. Heat treatment of several

## Page 133

of the mussel shells indicates human activity. Nevertheless, the sparse amount of cultural material recovered does not promise to add new information to the current understanding of the prehistoric inhabitants of the upper Trinity River basin. Trenching at site 41DL337 determined the area to be heavily impacted from past activities relating to the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant, including the construction of the settling pond immediately south of the area identified as the site, as well as the outflow channel to the north of the site. Due to the lack of cultural features, cultural artifacts, and the sparse nature of the shell remains, combined with the extensive disturbances that have been documented, site 41DL337 is recommended ineligible for inclusion in the NRHP. SITE 41DL338 Previous Investigations Site 41DL338 was also recorded by S. Alan Skinner of AR Consultants on September 9, 1992, in conjunction with the construction of the effluent outflow channel on the northeast side of the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant (Skinner and Whorton 1995:15). The site was discovered along the cutbank on the west side of the outflow channel then under construction and directly north of the small east-west drainage channel that empties into the outflow channel. It was reported to contain a_ shell lens approximately 3 m below ground surface that extended for approximately 3 m along the bank. Monitoring of the site for more than a year revealed only mussel shell and, ultimately, no cultural material was exposed in the bank. Skinner concluded that the site was in a derived context (Skinner and Whorton 1995:15). The site was revisited by GMI personnel on May 14, 1997. However, high water levels precluded any investigation of the site at that time. The lower water level (2.5 m below ground surface) on August 5, 1997, allowed GMI personnel access to the site. The area mapped as site 115 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites 41DL338 was heavily overgrown by weeds, which made investigation of the cutbank area difficult. Examination of a large area of the bank failed to produce any indication of the cultural remains originally reported at the site. The buried concrete channel perpendicular to the outflow channel that separates sites 41DL337 and 41DL338 was located, but no evidence of cultural material was identified. At the time it was felt that, given the water level (2.5 m below surface) and the reported depth of the site G m below surface), the cultural material was permanently below water level in the channel. Nevertheless, as discussed above, the potential was believed to exist for site 41DL338 to yield data relevant to prehistoric research issues in Northcentral Texas, including paleoenvironmental reconstruction, culture history, settlement-subsistence systems, lithic raw material use patterns, and prehistoric technology. Consequently, it was recommended that the site be considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP pending further investigations (Cliff et al. 1998). Current Investigations Five trackhoe trenches (Trenches 29, 31, 32, 33, and 37) were excavated at site 41DL338 (Table 34; see Figure 28). In order to expose the cultural deposits identified by Skinner, four of the five trackhoe trenches were excavated along the edge of the water, perpendicular to the effluent outflow channel. Trench 29 was placed in the area originally recorded as site 41DL338, directly north of the old effluent channel that exits the Waste Water Treatment Plant from southwest to northeast. This trench exposed one of the settling ponds used during the early years of the treatment plant. Fill material included plastic fragments, concrete, milled wood, and glass fragments, and was underlain by decaying organic matter composed primarily of branches and roots to the maximum depth of the trench at 305 cm bs. Trenches 31, 32, and 33 were excavated at approximately 20-m intervals and extended north from Trench 29. Trenches 31

Trench 37 was excavated to the west of the other trenches, parallel to the outflow channel. The placement of this trench was designed to identify the relationship between the identified paleosol and the disturbance caused by the settling pond. The upper 2 m of the trench contained plastic and paper fragments as well as decaying organic matter. The clay layer beneath the disturbance was extremely mottled and produced no mussel shells or other cultural material. Trench 37 was excavated to 380 cm bs with no evidence of the typical 1OYR 3/1 dark gray brown soil of the West Fork. This suggested that the paleosol had been truncated during construction of the settling pond. Only Trenches 31 and 32 yielded any indication of the deposit identified by Skinner as site 41DL338. Trench 31 revealed primarily small shell fragments between 105 and 210 cm bs, although two complete shells were recovered. Shell was retrieved from Trench 32 between 105 and 230 cm bs (Table 35). Overall, the shells from Trench 32 were more complete than those recovered from Trench 31. In addition, a small 116 amount of burned clay was observed between 200 and 220 cm bs. No bone or charcoal were observed during trenching activities at site 41DL338. The north side of Trench 32 was cleared to a depth of approximately 140 cm bs, and Unit 8 was initiated on the northeast edge of the trench. This feature consisted of over 100 complete mussel shells and a small amount of charcoal. No other level excavated produced nearly the amount of shell as the 160- to-170-cm level. Because of the small amount of shell recovered at lower depths, the unit was reduced to a .5-x-.5-m unit at 240 cm bs. Excavation of the .5-x-.5-m unit proceeded to 300 cm bs with continued decreasing amounts of mussel shell. Trenching at sites 41DL338 and 41DL355 revealed that the area directly west, southwest, and northwest of 41DL338 served as a settling pond that was later filled and planted with saplings. Trenches 29 and 33, located approximately 20 m southeast and northwest of the intact soils identified in Trenches 31 and 32, revealed fill material to depths of 305 and 190 cm bs, respectively. However, at least a portion of site 41DL338 appears to have remained relatively intact, despite the disturbance of the settling pond and the excavation of the outflow channel directly east of the site. The sediments revealed in Trenches 31 and 32 exhibit a C-AC-Abca profile Figures 34 and 35; see Appendix F). The C horizon was divided into two subhorizons—C and 2C (or 2C1). The upper subhorizon (C) consists of 20 to 25 cm of brown (10YR4/3 to 10YR 5/3) clayey silt exhibiting a massive, plastic structure. The underlying subhorizon @C or 2Cl) was characterized by dark grayish brown (2.5Y 4/2 or 10YR 4/2) clay, also with a massive, plastic structure; this subhorizon contained abundant orange mottles typical of disturbed soils within the project area. ## Page 138

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas represent back fill from the construction of the settling pond. A clear boundary distinguished the C horizon from the underlying zone. Thickness of the C horizon ranged from 70 cm thick in Trench 31 to 50 cm in Trench 32. The AC horizon was revealed only in Trench 32 and represents a very late Holocene soil development consisting of a mineral horizon of parent material (C) with an accumulation of humified organic matter (A); the mineral portion of the horizon is dominant. This horizon is characterized by a dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) clay with orange mottling in the lower part of the horizon; it exhibits a coarse blocky structure, heavy clay skins on ped faces, and common calcareous deposits along rootlet casts: The AC horizon lightens with increasing depth, the boundary of which is gradual to diffuse. As at site 41DL337, the irregularity of the AC horizon, or in this case complete lack of an AC horizon in all but Trench 32, is likely due to disturbances, particularly since the C horizon in Trench 31 appeared to be fill. There was no clear indication that the AC horizon in Trench 32 itself was fill; however, several fragments of purplish, decaying organic matter were identified. The West Fork soil was encountered in Trenches 31, 32, and 37 at depths that ranged from 70 cm (Trench 31) to 250 cm bs (Trench 37; see Figures 34 and 35). The B horizon of the West Fork was not observed in the trenching activities at site 41DL338, although test unit excavations in Trench 32 encountered the B horizon at approximately 225 cm bs (see below). The A horizon of the West Fork consists of very dark grayish brown to very dark gray (2.5Y 3/2 to 10YR 3/1) clay with a strong, blocky structure, heavy clay skins on ped faces, and common calcareous deposits along rootlet casts. Other soil characteristics of the West Fork A horizon vary among the three trenches—Trenches 31 and 37 contained small calcareous concretions, Trench 37 exhibited evidence of gleying, while Trench 32 exhibited few calcareous concretions and no evidence of gleying. As discussed above, the irregular depths of the upper boundary of the 120 West Fork A horizon in the three trenches likely resulted from the construction of the settling pond, Figures 36 and 37 clearly illustrate the extent of the disturbances within the limited area identified as site 41DL338. Horizontal and Vertical Extent Based on the excavation of five trenches, two of which were positive, site 41DL338 measures approximately 26 m northwest to southeast by 23 m southwest to northeast. Although this estimate is based solely on the basis of two trenches, it should be considered relatively accurate. The size of the site is limited primarily by the extensive disturbances identified. Trenches 29, 33, and 37 all revealed fill deposits, while the recently constructed outflow channel is located directly northeast of Trenches 31 and 32. No shell remains were identified in the outflow channel cut bank or in the shallow water below the cutbank. Because only two trenches yielded cultural material, analysis of horizontal variability at site 41DL338 is necessarily limited. Table 36 summarizes the recovery from the trenches. Trench 32 yielded twice as much shell by weight as Trench 31, while both trenches produced a single piece of burned clay. Since the shell from Trench 32 was generally more complete than that recovered from Trench 31, hand excavations were initiated adjacent to Trench 32. Two units were excavated adjacent to Trench 32—a 1-x-1-m unit (Unit 8) and a .5-x-.5-m unit (Unit 9). Unit 8 was reduced to a .5-x-.5-m below 220 cm bs because of time constraints and a lack of features or lithic artifacts. Unit 10, a .5-x-.5-m unit, was excavated adjacent to Trench 31. Unit excavations and profile discussions are detailed below. Generally, Table 37 illustrates the extreme variability in the quantity of mussel shell recovered in the three units. Units 8 and 9 were excavated only 1 m apart beside the same trench. However the amount of shell these two units yielded varied significantly. ## Page 141

The vertical and horizontal distribution of materials recovered from site 41DL338 indicate at least three occupation zones. The most recent appears to be associated with the shell concentration from Feature 5 (160 to 170 cm bs), which begins 10 cm into the West Fork soil. Though still a large quantity of shell compared to the other test units in this project, the concentration directly above Feature 5 is proportionately smaller and the shells are less intact, which suggests that their location resulted from bioturbation or decreased use of the area over time. In addition, shells from both levels indicate heat treatment (see Non-Artifactual Remains discussion below). However, it is also possible that this shell accumulation at the top of the West Fork soil is related to a later occupation zone that was destroyed by construction activities related to the Waste Water Treatment Plant. Nevertheless, although some decaying material was encountered in the overlying AC horizon in Trench 32, there is no distinct evidence that this stratum represents a disturbance. The considerable amount of shell recovered 40 to 110 cm into the West Fork soil (190 and 260 cm bs) suggests intensive, albeit short-term, use of this area over a long period of time, although the focus of utilization appears to be 60 to 90 cm into the West Fork (10 to 240 cm bs). Heat-altered shells are evident at 220 to 230 cm bs. The third recognizable zone is evident in Unit 8 from 120 cm into the West Fork soil to the bottom of the unit (70 to 300 cm bs), although no indication of heat treatment of these shells exists. Much smaller amounts of mussel shell are evident in the first 20 cm of the West Fork soil (190 to 210 cm bs) of Unit 9 and throughout much of the excavated portion of Unit 10. Both units indicate disturbances in the horizons above the West Fork soil, and these disturbances might have impacted the upper portions of the West Fork soil in these units. Nonetheless, Unit 9 was excavated adjacent to Unit 10 and, although much less dense, the distribution pattern of shell within the first 30 cm of the West Fork soil (190 to 220 cm bs) corresponds with the first observable occupation zone of Unit 8. Unit 10, 123 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites on the other hand, is more distant from Unit 8, and the West Fork soil, which may have already been truncated toward the upper depths, begins at a higher level than the other two units. Although it is possible that the small concentration of shell extending 40 to 70 cm into the West Fork soil (120 to 150 cm bs) relates to the upper portion of the proposed second zone of occupation in Unit 8, site disturbances make interpretation difficult. Unit 8 Excavations began with a .5-x-.5-m unit (Unit 8) in the eastern end of the cleared area at Trench 8 (Figure 36). The datum at Trench 32 was placed at 140 cm bs, while the first excavated level in Unit 8 was 150 to 160 cm bs. Figure 37 illustrates the north wall profile for Unit 8. Soil zones encountered in Unit 8 included less than 10 cm of the AC to C soil, followed by the more organic A subzone of the West Fork at 155 to 165 cm bs. The final soil horizon encountered was that of the lighter B subzone of the West Fork at a depth of 225 cm bs. The first 10-cm level yielded a considerable amount of shell, most of which appeared to be complete valves (see Table 38). Due to the concentration of mussel shell, it was decided to immediately open the excavations to a 1-x-l-m unit. The second 10-cm level revealed over 100 shells, 80 percent of which were complete valves. Identified as Feature 5, the soil matrix from around the shell concentration was removed and the remaining shells were sketched and photographed (see Feature 5 below). Following documentation, the shells from the 160 to 170-cm level were removed and excavation of the unit continued. The shell concentration consisted of over 2,500 g of mussel shell. The amount of shell in the levels below Feature 5 decreased significantly to 32.5 g, 7.0 g, and 31.7 g in the next three levels, respectively. The amount of shell increased again between 210 and 240 cm bs, although not to the extent of Feature 5. Bone was recovered between 170 and 180 and again between 190 and 200 cm bs. It is of interest that Feature 5 was identified at the very top of the West Fork, while

Unit 9 was terminated at 220 cm bs when only small fragments of mussel shell were encountered and deeper excavation of the .5-x-.5-m unit became difficult due to its small size. > Unit 10 Unit 10 was excavated adjacent to Trench 31 in order to sample the deposit identified in this area during trenching activities. The datum was established at a depth of 50 cm bs and the unit was started at 80 cm bs, at the top of the West Fork soil. Brown (10YR 5/3) sand-filled krotovina were observed in the upper 10 to 40 cm of the unit. This disturbance undoubtedly affected the distribution of artifacts and/or ecofacts in this area (Figure 39). A total of 11.7 g of mussel shell and a single fragment of burned clay weighing less than 0.1 g were recovered from Unit 10. The shell was distributed throughout the excavation in small quantities. The unit was terminated at 150 cm bs because of the absence of features, artifacts, or shell concentrations. Cultural Features Feature 5 Feature 5, the only cultural feature identified at site 41DL338, consisted of over 100 complete mussel shells and a small amount of charcoal. No associated burned clay or artifacts were observed (Figure 40). While a considerable amount of shell (187.3 g) was recovered in the level above the feature (150 to 160 cm bs), the - shell exposed in the 160 to 170-cm bs level was 126 more dense and intact. Several shells from each of these levels indicated heat treatment (see Non- Artifactual Remains discussion below). The shells were tightly embedded in the clay and adjacent to one another from the northern to the southern corner of the 1-x-l-m unit (orientation was to trench, not to a compass point). The concentration spanned the entire unit and was scattered throughout its western half. No shells were observed east of the heavy, diagonal concentration of shell (see Figure 40). Four fragments of charcoal that weighed 1.0 g were collected from the center of the unit (see Table 37). Radiocarbon dating analysis of the charcoal (Beta-129637) resulted in a date of 480 + 40 B.P. (see Radiocarbon Dating below). The top of Feature 5 was encountered 10 cm into the West Fork soil and extended down another 10 cm. Over 2,500 g of shell were collected from the feature, while only 32.5 g were recovered from the following 10-cm level. Aside from the charcoal, no other ecofacts were identified with the mussel shell feature. Two fragments of animal bone were recovered from the level below the feature, but none was associated with the feature itself. No burned clay associated with the shell concentration or the charcoal was observed, not even as specks in the soil as was noted at sites 41DL337 and 41DL356. In general, burned clay appears to be comparatively rare at site 41DL338. A soil sample from the north corner of Feature 5 was collected for flotation. This sample measured approximately 30 by 50 cm and approximately 10 cm in depth. Recovery from the sample consisted of mussel shell and small fragments of bone and burned clay (Table 40). No lithic artifacts were present. Non-Artifacual Remains Fourteen faunal specimens were recovered from site 41DL338 (see Appendix C). All were from Unit 8. An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas Table 40 Contents of Flotation Sample, Feature 5, Site 41DL338 Volume of Soil Provenience Bag No. Floated (1) Feature 5 Unit 8, 160-170 cm bs 180 23 unidentifiable mammal bone and three large mammal bones. Most of the faunal specimens were recovered from 190 to 200 cm bs, although two of the large mammal bones occurred between 170 and 180 cm bs. A rib head from a fish was discovered in a flotation sample from the 160 to 170-cm bs level, the same level as Feature 5. AlJl other remains coincide with smaller shell deposits. All material may have been related to prehistoric subsistence. Three species of terrestrial gastropods (n=52) and nine species of freshwater bivalves (n=458) were identified at this site (see Appendix E). The species of mollusks recovered are similar to those that currently inhabit the area and suggest that the environment was similar to that of the present. Specifically, the gastropods indicate riparian woodlands with adjacent open, grassy areas and a climate of hot, dry summers and relatively mild winters and the bivalves indicate a stream or river environment. Much of the bivalve assemblage was likely utilized as a food resource as indicated by the presence of 309 heat treated specimens. Heat treatment indicates human activity because the bivalves were processed with hot water or steam as opposed to charring, which can occur naturally and will dissolve or significantly shrivel the body inside the shell. One charred specimen, which could indicate burning after the meat was removed, was recovered from Trench 32. All heat treated specimens were recovered from Trench 32, and most were from Unit 8, particularly Feature 5, which yielded the most shell. The overwhelming majority of the specimens were immature. This suggests a 130 Mussel Shell Bone Burned Clay Wt. (g) Wt. (g) Wt. (g) 41.7 <0.1 0.2 constant collecting pressure was exerted on the local bivalve populations. The presence of one specimen, Sphaerium striatinum or the finger- nail clam, resides in shallow bodies of water and, therefore, indicates that at least some of the specimens could have been flood deposited. No evidence was found for the use of shells for tools or ornaments. Special Analyses Unit Flotation Samples In addition to the sample collected for flotation from Feature 5, flotation samples were also collected from Unit 8. As there was a potential for material smaller than the standard 6.4-mm (4-in) hardware cloth utilized during the excavations, a series of .008 m’* samples was collected from the top of Unit 8 to the bottom (Table 41). These samples were taken from the north wall of the unit. Mussel shell, bone, and burned clay fragments were recovered from these samples. No lithic material was present. The overall density of mussel shell in the floated samples from Unit 8 (1790.6 per m*) was lower than the density of both the 1-x-1-m (3901.0 per m?) and .5-x-.5-m (4206.7 per m’*) units (see Tables 38 and 41). The primary reason for this discrepancy is because the density of shell identified in Feature 5 (160 to 170 cm bs) was not present in the area sampled for flotation. The density levels in Table 40 clearly identify three peaks in the amount of mussel sheli. The first peak is that between 150 and 160 cm bs, the level above Feature 5. The second is between

Radiocarbon Dating One charcoal sample (Beta-129637), from Feature 5, was submitted for radiocarbon dating and produced a date of 480 + 40 BP. (see Appendix G). This date corresponds with the Late Prehistoric II period in the upper Trinity River basin. However, the sample was taken from a context 10-15 cm below the surface of the West Fork soil. Therefore, the date only applies to the most recent prehistoric occupation of the site. Summary Trackhoe trenches and test units excavated at site 41DL338 indicate the largest accumulation of bivalve shell in the project area. Small amounts of charcoal, bone, and burned clay were 131 Bone Burned Clay Wt. (g) ~~ Density Wt. (g) Density (per m*) (per m*) 0.1 12.5 <0.1 <12.5 0.3 37.5 0.1 12.5 <0.1 16 0.5 7.8 recovered, although no lithics were found. At least three vertically sequential concentrations of shell, including one shell feature, were observable and suggest fairly intense, though short-term, occupation of the site over a long period of time. Several heat-treated molluscan specimens were identified and indicate human activity. Smaller amounts of other faunal, particularly aquatic, remains were recovered and may also be by-products of human subsistence activity. A charcoal sample from the upper level of the paleosol generated a radiocarbon date of 480 + 40 B.P. (Late Prehistoric II period) for one of the more recent zones of site utilization. However, no potential for obtainmg new knowledge about prehistoric human activity in the upper Trinity River basin exists and destruction of the site from settling pond excavations by the Wastewater Treatment Plant have compromised the site’s integrity. Therefore, it is recommended that site 41DL338 be considered ineligible for the NRHP. SITE 41DL355 Previous Investigations Site 41DL355 was recorded by S. Alan Skinner of AR Consultants on September 8, 1993, during the construction of the effluent outflow channel


![Image 1 - Page 149](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p149_img1.png)

## Page 150

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas on the northeast side of the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant (Skinner and Whorton 1995:16). The site was located 3 m below ground surface on the west bank of the 1992 outflow channel, between the original effluent outflow channel and the outflow box of the more recent channel. Cultural material observed at the site at that time included primarily mussel shell, with some mammal bone fragments (probably deer), and fire-cracked limestone. The material was discovered in a deposit approximately 25 cm thick that extended approximately 11 m along the water’s edge. Skinner identified the occupation as Late Archaic or Late Prehistoric, although no diagnostic artifacts were observed. GMI personnel attempted to visit the site on May 14, 1997; however, the high water level prevented access. The site was reexamined by GMI personnel on August 5, 1997, at which time the water level was 2.5 m below ground surface. The area of site 41DL355 was heavily overgrown with vegetation, and examination of a large area along the east bank of the outflow channel failed to relocate any of the cultural remains identified during the original recording of the site. As with site 41DL338, it appears that any cultural remains that may be present were inundated by the water in the outflow channel. In light of site 41DL355’s proximity and possible connection with sites 41DL337, 41DL338, and 41DL356, it was believed that it had a potential to yield data that is relevant to prehistoric research issues in Northcentral Texas, including paleoenvironmental reconstruction, culture history, settlement-subsistence systems, lithic raw material use patterns, and prehistoric technology. Consequently, it was recommended that the site be considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP pending further investigations (Cliff et al. 1998). Current Investigations Four trenches (Trenches 30, 34, 38, and 39) were excavated in the area identified by Skinner as site 41DL355 (Table 42; see Figure 28). The 132 trenches were positioned approximately 10 m apart and perpendicular to the effluent outflow channel, directly northwest of the concrete outflow box and south of the old effluent channel exiting the plant from southwest to northeast. All four trenches excavated at site 41DL355 exposed the same settling pond deposits identified at site 411DL338 to the north. Disturbed soils were exposed to a depth of approximately 300 to 320 cm bs and consisted of a heavily mottled orange and gray clay with abundant decaying organics and modern material that included road asphalt, concrete, burned milled wood, plastic, and glass. Several complete mussel shells and mussel shell fragments were observed throughout the disturbed zone but were not present in the apparently undisturbed clay encountered below 300 to 320 cm bs (Table 43). This suggested that the shell was present only in disturbed contexts. A l0YR 3/2 clay that exhibited characteristics of the West Fork soil was encountered in Trench 39 at a depth of 210 cm bs; however, this horizon also contained plastic fragments, including an antihistamine nasal spray bottle. In addition, a layer of burned limestone fragments was encountered at 230 cm bs in Trench 38. The West Fork soil appears to have been completely disturbed in this area, probably during treatment plant activities related to the earlier settling ponds and the construction of outflow channels. Sediment and Stratigraphy Located directly southeast of site 41DL338, the area previously identified as site 41DL355 is also mapped as being frequently flooded Trinity clay. It is directly north of the area identified as disturbed because of the removal of limestone or shale bedrock and south of an area identified as Arents (loamy, hilly) that consists of the remaining overburden from gravel mining that has been distributed in mounds and ridges in former gravel pits (USDA, SCS 1980). As discussed above, trenching at sites 41DL338 and 41DL355 revealed that, with the exception of

Fill was revealed in all four trenches (Trenches 30, 34, 38, and 39) excavated at site 41DL355. The sediments in the area recorded as site 41DL355 are characterized by fill, followed by a disturbed Abcag soil horizon (Figure 41 and Appendix F). The deposits identified as fill extended to a depth of at least 300 to 340 cm bs, and included a C horizon below the fill (clay with concrete and asphalt fragments, plastic, decaying lumber, and glass fragments; see Figure 41). The C horizon was characterized by dark grayish brown (2.5Y 3/2) clay with heavy clay skins and abundant orange mottles. Abundant blackish-purple decaying organic matter and a thick layer of rotting grass and weeds, as well as a strong methane odor, indicate this layer too is primarily fill. In addition to the settling pond and later outflow channel activity, the disturbances could also be attributed to the construction of the old outflow channel that flows southwest to northeast and is evidenced today by a small concrete-lined channel between sites 41DL338 and 41DL355. The Abcag horizon was encountered in Trenches 30, 38, and 39 at depths that ranged from 250 cm bs (Trench 38) to 340 cm bs (Trench 39; see Figure 41). This horizon consisted of very dark grayish brown (2.5Y 3/2) clay that exhibited a blocky structure with clay skins on ped faces. Calcareous concretions were common and occasional evidence of gleying was observed in all three trenches. Evidence of disturbance was also identified in this horizon in the form of plastic fragments and green carpet fibers at a depth of approximately 340 cm bs in Trench 39. Although the water table prevented deeper excavations in this area, the excavations indicated no evidence of the typical West Fork soil deposits identified at sites 41DL337, 41DL338, and 41DL356. Extensive disturbances appear to have completely removed the West Fork paleosol, and with it any in situ cultural remains that may have been present. 133

## Page 153

Summary Trenching activities determined the that area previously identified as site 41DL355 was heavily impacted from past activities related to the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant. These activities include the construction of a settling pond immediately west of the area identified as the site, as well as the outflow channel immediately to the east. Although mussel shell remains were identified in the trenches excavated at site 41DL355, these remains were present only in zones identified as heavily disturbed and/or fill material. Investigations at site 41DL355 indicated that the activities associated with the waste water treatment plant completely destroyed the West Fork soil in the area identified as site 41DL355, and that the shell remains present in this area are in redeposited soils. Due to the lack of an intact cultural horizon, it is recommended that site 41DL355 be considered ineligible for inclusion in the NRHP. SITE 41DL356 Previous Investigations Site 41DL356 was recorded by S. Alan Skinner of AR Consultants on September 8, 1992, during the construction of the effluent outflow channel on the northeast side of the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant (Skinner and Whorton 1995:16). The site was located on the east bank of the outflow channel then under construction and across the channel from site 41DL355. Site 41DL356 was reported to consist of two shell lenses exposed approximately 3 m below ground surface and separated by approximately 50 cm of black clay. The shell lenses were exposed for approximately 20 m along the bank of the outflow channel. In addition to the mussel shell, the presence of mammal bones, probably deer, was noted on the site form. Skinner identified the occupation as Late Archaic or Late Prehistoric, although no diagnostic artifacts were observed. — 135 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites The site was revisited by GMI personnel on May 14, 1997; however, high water levels precluded any investigation of the site at that time. The lower water level on August 5, 1997 (2.5 m below ground surface), allowed GMI personnel access to the site. The area mapped as site 41DL355 was heavily overgrown by weeds and examination of the cutbank area was difficult. An in situ. shell lens was _ identified approximately 160 cm bs, and several large shells were visible beneath the water surface in the channel below the site. The shells observed under water appeared to be larger than those observed along the channel bank. The crew was unable to relocate the second shell lens identified by Skinner, although this lens may be represented by the shells observed underwater. As discussed above, investigations elsewhere in the upper Trinity River basin (i.e., sites 41TR138 and 41CO150) suggest a common pattern of dispersed, low density activity areas and shell lenses associated with a single site area. On the basis of an in situ shell lens at site 41DL356 and the proximity of the other three sites, the potential exists for each of these sites to yield data relevant to prehistoric research issues in Northcentral Texas, including paleoenvironmental reconstruction, culture history, settlement-subsistence systems, lithic raw material use patterns, and prehistoric technology. Consequently, it was recommended that site 41DL356 be considered eligible for inclusion in the NRHP pending further investigations (Cliff et al. 1998). Current Investigations Six trenches were excavated in the area identified as site 41DL356, all of which were positioned perpendicular to the outflow channel (Table 44; see Figure 28). Four of the trenches (Trenches 17, 20, 36, and 40) were excavated along the water’s edge, while the remaining two trenches (Trenches 18 and 19) were positioned to the east of the first four in order to determine the eastern boundary of the site. However, the West Fork soil, encountered at a depth of approximately 110 cm bs, appeared to be intact. Mussel shell was recovered between 120 and 170 cm bs and a concentration of burned clay (Feature 3) was revealed in the bottom of the trench at a depth of 160 cm bs (Table 45). While Trench 17 did produce some mussel shell fragments, the disturbed zone above 220 cm bs and the seepage of water into the trench at approximately 250 cm bs precluded further excavation in this trench. Similarly, Trench 20 exhibited mussel shell mixed with modern material and decaying organics. The water table was encountered at 240 cm bs. No intact soils were observed in Trench 20 and excavation was terminated at 380 cm bs. Trenches 18 and 19, excavated east of the other four trenches revealed another settling pond used during the 136 early years of the treatment plant. The excavations revealed highly organic, black soil with decaying roots and branches capped by a layer of boulder- and cobble-sized limestone fragments at approximately 100 cm bs. The organic layer extended to the maximum depths of the trenches at 250 and 300 cm bs, respectively. Therefore, Trench 36 was the only trench of the original five trenches excavated that was left open. Because excavation of this trench was terminated at what appeared to be the upper layers of the cultural horizon (160 cm bs), Trench 40 was excavated approximately 5 m south of Trench 36 to determine the vertical extent of the West Fork soil and the culture- bearing deposit. While, the top of the West Fork was revealed at 110 cm bs in Trench 36, it was encountered at 160 cm bs in Trench 40, which indicated that the depth of the West Fork increased to the south. A shell lens was also encountered in Trench 40 at 160 cm bs. A lighter gray, more clayey soil was revealed below 210 cm bs and the excavation of the trench was terminated before the water table was encountered.

## Page 156

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas Unit 7 was excavated adjacent to Trench 40 and extended to 250 cm. It produced small amounts of shell fragments throughout the excavation. No bummed clay was observed and no concentrations of shell were encountered. A single bone was recovered between 180 and 190 cm bs and a burned root was observed between 210 and 220 cm bs. r Finally, Units 11 and 12, together forming a .5- x-l-m unit, were excavated between Feature 3 in the bottom of Trench 36 and Units 3 and 5 (Features 1 and 2). While scattered fragments of shell and burned clay were recovered from both units, no concentrations of either material were identified in these units. Fragments of bone were recovered between 180 and 220 cm bs. Excavation of the units terminated at 220 cm bs when the amount of shell continued to decrease and the lighter gray, clayey soil was encountered. Sediment and Stratigraphy The area identified as site 41DL356 is mapped as consisting of frequently flooded Trinity clay. It is directly adjacent to an area identified as disturbed due to the removal of limestone. or shale bedrock (USDA, SCS 1980). Trenching has revealed that the area directly east of the site was used as a settling pond, probably during the early years of the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant, and later filled and planted with saplings. The area directly west of site 41DL356 is now occupied by the current 30-m wide outflow channel. At least a portion of site 41DL356 appears to have remained relatively intact, despite the disturbances surrounding the site. The sediments at site 41DL356 are characterized by a C-Ab/AC-Ab profile (Figure 42 and Appendix F). The C horizon consists of 20 to 45 cm of recent fluvial deposits. The upper level of this horizon consists of finely bedded yellowish brown (10YR 5/4), fine grained sand and dark to very dark grayish brown (1OYR 4/2 to 3/2) silty clay. A lower C horizon was 138 identified in.Trenches 17 and 20 and consists of fill and disturbed sediments including burned branches and roots, plastic, decaying paper, glass bottles, and a cow leg bone. This horizon appears to represent spoil dug from either the outflow channel or the settling pond. The C horizon boundary is clear to gradual. The Ab to AC horizon is characterized by a dark to very dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2 to 10YR 3/2) clay with orange mottling that exhibits a coarse blocky structure and clay skins on ped faces. Orange mottling and dark decaying material were identified throughout this horizon. The Ab to AC horizon was identified only in Trenches 36 and 40 and was 90 to 110 cm in thickness. The zone darkens with depth to a very dark grayish brown (1OYR 3/2) with less mottling. The boundary of this Ab to AC horizon is very gradual to gradual. The Ab to AC horizon itself exhibited some evidence of disturbance in the upper 40 to 60 cm—dark decaying organic matter and plastic fragments were observed. The extensive disturbance identified in nearby trenches rendered it unclear exactly how much of this clay-rich horizon was in situ. Furthermore, the sandy silty nature of the fluvial deposits overlying the AC horizon suggested that currents during periods of flooding were of sufficient energy to erode pre- existing deposits (see Appendix F). The disturbance noted in the upper levels of the AC horizon did indicate, however, that the disturbances associated with construction of the outflow channel or the settling pond occurred before the accumulation of the interbedded fluvial deposits (horizon C) and toward the end of the accumulation of the clay-rich AC horizon. The next soil horizon identified in the trench excavations was that of the West Fork soil—a thick, dark clay and calcium-rich cumulic soil that grades slowly into its parent material (see Appendix F). Only the A subhorizon of the West Fork was reached in the trenches excavated at site 41DL337. The B horizon (B or Bea) of the West Fork soil was encountered in the excavation of the test units (see below). The A horizon of the West Fork consists of very dark

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas gray (10YR 3/1) clay with a strong, blocky structure. Common characteristics include calcareous deposits along rootlet casts, fine vertical sand-filled pockets, particularly in the upper zone of the horizon, and fewer calcareous concretions than in the AC horizon. The A horizon was encountered at a depth of approximately 220 cm bs in Trench 17, 105 to 117 cm bs in Trench 36, and 150 cm bs in Trench 40. The B horizon of the West Fork was observed in each of the seven units excavated at the site. It consists of a dark gray brown to brown (10YR 4/1 to 10YR 4/3) clay with coarse to fine blocky structure. Silt coats are common on ped faces, while there is an increase in the amount of calcareous concretions from the A horizon. The B horizon was reached between 190 cm bs (Units 3 through 6) and 225 cm bs (Unit 7). All West Fork sediments appeared intact in Trenches 36 and 40. A slight variation of the depth of the West Fork was identified at site 41DL356, evidenced by the increased depth of the paleosol in Trench 40 and Unit 7 (see Figure 42). The east-west cross section of site 41DL356 in Figure 43 illustrates the extent and proximity of the disturbance that resulted from the creation of the settling pond south of the site. Trench 18 was excavated approximately 18 m east of Trench 36 and revealed fill that consisted of limestone fragments, abundant decaying organic matter, and what appeared to be a rock cap, directly below the surface. The clay-rich Ab to AC horizon and the West Fork soil encountered in the trenches directly to the west had been completely removed. Horizontal and Vertical Extent Based on the testing program conducted at site 41DL356, the site measures approximately 20 m north-south and 35 m_ east-west. Site measurements were based on two positive trenches (Trenches 36 and 40) that contained intact cultural deposits, as well as the presence of mussel shells in the shallow water of the outflow 4 140 channel. Site boundaries were limited by the settling pond identified in Trenches 18 and 19 to the east, and disturbed deposits and fill identified in Trenches 17 and 20 to the south and north of the site, respectively (see Figure 28). Table 46 summarizes the recovery from the trench excavations at site 41DL356. Trench 40, excavated approximately 4 m south of Trench 36, produced the most mussel shell remains, whereas Trench 36 produced the most burned clay remains. Feature 3 was comprised almost entirely by the burned clay encountered in the bottom of Trench 36. With the exception of mussel shell, very few ecofactual remains were recovered from the trenching activities. Because the intact cultural deposits at site 41DL356 were limited to a very small area, the entire area between Trenches 36 and 40, as well as the area north of Trench 36, was opened in a series of terraces prior to excavation of the test units (Figure 44). The terracing approach was adopted due to the gradual increase in the depth of the West Fork toward the south. The area north of Trench 36 was cleared to 70 cm bs, the area directly south of Trench 36 was cleared to 90 cm bs, and because the mussel shells observed in Trench 40 were at approximately 150 cm bs, the area directly north of Trench 40 was cleared to that depth. This allowed the seven .5-x-.5-m units to be scattered over an area measuring approximately 4 x 5 m (see Figure 44), Units 3 and 5 were excavated adjacent to one another on the southern edge of Trench 36; Units 4 and 6 were excavated directly north of Trench 36; Units 11 and 12 were positioned to test the area between Units 3 and 5 and Feature 3; and finally, Trench 7 was excavated directly north of Trench 40. Overall, Unit 7 produced the most mussel shell of the seven units excavated, although the shell collected from Unit 7 was not embedded in clay and adjacent to other shells as was observed in Features 3 and 4 at this site and Feature 5 at site 41DL338 (Table 47). The shell from Unit 7 was generally more abundant than in the other units, but was not identified as a

Generally, it could be stated that the most productive area of the site is the western edge of the tested area. However, it must be stated that the original size of this site, prior to disturbance by construction of the outflow channel and the settling pond, is not known; _ therefore, conclusions about the horizontal variability at site 41DL356 are limited. When the distribution of shell and other remains are compared, however, it appears the most intensive use of the site, as evidenced by a significant increase in the amount of mussel shell, does not coincide with an increase in burned clay, charcoal, or bone (see Table 47). Approximately 66 percent (5.8 g) of the bone from the units was recovered between 20 and 30 cm above the shell increase observed between 160 and 210 cm bs. Thirty-two percent (2.8 g) of the bone was recovered from below 200 cm. None of the bone was recovered from the levels that produced the most mussel shell. Similarly, only 29 percent (0.2 g) of the charcoal and less than one percent (0.2 g) of the burned clay was recovered from those levels. This would not be unexpected if the site was habitually used for brief periods of time. Like site 41DL338, the overall distribution of shell and burned clay suggests continuous site utilization over a long period of time with an extremely high concentration occurring at a similar level throughout the test units on the site. In addition to this zone of intense utilization (ca. 60 cm into the West Fork soil), a lower density concentration was present 10 to 30 cm into the paleosol of nearly all test units excavated at 41DL356. A third increase in shell density 145 occurs at 80 cm and 120 cm into the West Fork soil in Units 3 and 5, respectively. However, the other test units indicate a more constant amount of shell at these levels that decreases slightly with depth. The change in shell accumulations below the zone of peak usage in Units 3 and 5 more likely reflects variation in location of site use than a distinct period of occupation. ## Page 164

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas encountered between 165 and 170 cm bs, Unit 5 was placed between Unit 3 and Trench 36. Due to this placement, Unit 5 measures only 40 cm north to south and 50 cm east to west. The east wall profile of Units 3 and 5 is illustrated in Figure 45. The West Fork was encountered at depths of 130 to 140 cm bs in Unit 3 and 120 to 130 cm bs in Unit 5. The profile illustrates the descent of the depth of the West Fork toward the north by approximately 40 cm over a distance of 90 cm north to south. Almost twice as much shell was recovered from Unit 5 as Unit 3, whereas the burned clay feature was identified primarily in Unit 3 (see below). The upper Ab to AC zone produced few remains, as did the upper 20 cm of the West Fork (Ab-I) until a depth of 150 to 160 cm bs where a slight increase in mussel shell was noted. The greatest increase in shell was observed in Unit 5, 170 to 180 cm bs, the level just below the burned clay feature (Feature 1) identified at 170 cm bs in Unit 3. Feature 2, another bummed clay concentration, was identified between 150 and 153 cm bs in Unit 5. While an increase in the amount of shell was noted at this level in Unit 5, none of the shell was associated with the concentration of burned clay. Most of the bone from Unit 5 was recovered from the same level as the feature, although none of it was associated with the feature. The recovery in Unit 3 fluctuates throughout the profile, whereas Unit 5 exhibited two significant peaks, one at 170 to 180 cm bs and another at 240 to 250 cm bs (see Table 47). This last level corresponds to the B horizon of the West Fork paleosol identified by a slightly lighter soil (10YR3/2), an increase in the calcium carbonate inclusions and the clay content, and a less organic quality to the soil. Excavation of the units was terminated at 250 cm bs, primarily because excavation any deeper would have required expanding the unit to a l-x-l-m unit. It was decided that placement of additional units nearby, rather than adjacent to Units 3 and 5, would be more productive. 146 Units 4 and 6 Unit 4 was excavated north of Trench 36. Unit 5 was soon after placed adjacent to Unit 4 since experience with Units 3 and 5 indicated that at least a .5-x-1-m area would need to be opened if the excavations were to extend to a reasonable depth. The Ab to AC zone was relatively shallow in these units, primarily because the mechanical clearing of this area was conducted to remove most of the soil above the West Fork (Figure 46). The West Fork was encountered at 90 to 110 cm bs and was marked by a significant amount of dark grayish brown, silty-clay-filled krotovina. The krotovina were fine to small and probably represented root or insect disturbance. The dark grayish brown silty clay decreased below approximately 135 to 140 cm bs as the occurrence of mussel shell increased. A concentration of mussel shell, Feature 4, was identified between 155 and 170 cm bs and was primarily confined to Unit 6 (see below). No burned clay was associated with this feature. However, several specimens of the shell recovered from each level of Unit 6 indicated heat treatment or charring (see Non-Artifactual Remains discussion below). Aside from shell, no other material type was recovered from either unit until a small amount of bone was collected from the last level of Unit 6 (see Table 47). As with Units 3 and 5, Units 4 and 6 were terminated at 210 cm bs, when size of the unit limited further excavation. A series of soil samples was collected for flotation. Results are discussed below under Special Analyses. Unit 7 Unit 7 was placed closer to Trench 40 (see Figure 44) in order to sample the shell lens identified during the excavation of that trench. The terrace upon which the unit was placed had been excavated to the top of the West Fork; therefore, none of the Ab to AC zone was present (Figure 47). As with the other units, an

## Page 168

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas increase in shell was noted approximately 60 cm into the West Fork soil. Heat treated shell specimens were identified from 190 to 230 cm bs. Most were from the 220 to 230 level where the majority of the shell was recovered (see Non- Artifactual Remains discussion below). Burned roots were also observed in the paleosol. The unit was terminated at 245 cm bs, approximately 20 cm into the B horizon of the West Fork paleosol. Units 11 and 12 Units 11 and 12 were positioned between Feature 3 identified on the floor of Trench 36, Units 3 and 5 to the east, and Unit 7 to the southeast (see Figure 44). The placement of these units was intended to identify the extent of the burned clay and shell identified in Feature 3, as well as the increase in shell noted across the site at a depth of approximately 60 cm into the West Fork paleosol. As in Units 4 and 6, a significant amount of disturbance was observed in the upper levels, probably the result of root or insect activity (Figure 48). The shell increase was observed in Unit 11, although at slightly higher level (40 cm) than other units. The recovery from Unit 12 was more evenly distributed throughout the profile (see Table 47). Like Units 3, 5, and 6, the small amount of charcoal and burned clay recovered from Units 11 and 12 was from the levels above this shell increase. Ninety-four percent (1.7 g) of the bone, however, was recovered from the last level of Unit 12. Cultural Features Three cultural features were observed at site 41DL356. Feature 1 and 2 consisted of small, dispersed fragments of burned clay. Feature 3 was a large, concentrated area of burned clay with mussel shells underneath. Feature 4 was strictly a mussel shell concentration. Each of these features is detailed below. 150 Feature 1 This feature was encountered at 170 cm bs in Units 3 and 5 (Figure 49). Initially the feature was identified during the excavation of Unit 3, at which time Unit 5 was placed adjacent to Unit 3. During the excavation of Unit 5, Feature 2 was identified at 150 cm bs. Therefore, Feature 2 was completely documented before Feature 1 was fully exposed. Feature 1 was identified south of and approximately 20 cm below Feature 2. Feature 1 consisted of several small chunks of burned clay and several pieces of charcoal within a matrix of mixed dark brown to very dark gray C1OYR 3/2 to 3/1) clay. The surrounding matrix was primarily very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay. The feature measured approximately 35 cm by 18 cm and was no thicker than the approximately 2 cm that was exposed at the 170-cm bs level. The excavation of the next 10-cm level in Unit 3 revealed no profile. Compared to Feature 3 and the burned clay features identified at 41DL319/357, Feature 1 was very limited in size and content. It could be argued as to whether this scatter of burned clay fragments should be considered a feature at all, let alone a cultural feature. Furthermore, charcoal from this feature (Beta-129630) was radiocarbon dated to 970 + 40 B.P. (see Radiocarbon Dating discussion below). Single mussel shells were identified approximately 10 cm north and 35 cm northwest of the feature; however, there appeared to be no direct association between the shell and the burned clay. A very dark grayish brown (1OYR 4/2), clay-filled krotovina, approximately 3 cm wide and caused either by roots or rodent activity, was identified directly southeast of the feature. The feature was documented and excavation of Units 3 and 5 continued. Feature 2 Feature 2 was identified in Unit 5 at 150 cm bs (Figure 50). As discussed above, it was observed approximately 20 cm above Feature 1, which itself prompted the excavation of Unit 5. The feature was very similar to Feature 1 and,

## Page 172

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas thus, was unlike most of the other burned clay features identified in the project. The burned clay fragments that comprised the feature were small and scattered, unlike Feature 3 or the bummed clay features identified at 41DL319/357. The matrix directly surrounding the burned clay was mixed dark brown to very dark gray (1JOYR 3/2 to 3/1) clay. The matrix outside the area delimited as the feature was the very dark gray (1OYR 3/1) clay of the West Fork soil. Feature 2 measured approximately 13 cm northeast to southwest by 8.5 cm northwest to southeast and extended approximately 3 cm to 153 cm bs. No mussel shell was associated with the burned clay; however, a complete mussel shell valve was recovered from 155 cm bs approximately 75 cm south of the feature. Disturbance in the form of a root or animal burrow was noted near the shell, though the feature itself does not appear to have been disturbed. Soil from this feature was submitted for radiocarbon analysis (Beta-129631). The sample produced at date of 1,660 + 50 B.P., which is consistent with its depth into the West Fork soil. As with Feature 1, the potential cultural nature of this feature is in doubt since it was very shallow and the burned clay was sparse compared to other features. Feature 3 Feature 3 was identified during the excavation of Trench 36 at 160 cm bs and digging activity was halted. The feature consisted of three separate burned clay areas designated from east to west as Areas A, B, and C (Figure 51). Area C was the only portion of the feature identified during trenching activities. The other two were observed after the feature area was cleared by hand. Based on the amount of bumed soil recovered from the trackhoe bucket, it is estimated that an additional 8 to 10 cm of the feature was removed before excavations could be terminated. Both Area A and Area B were filled with brown (10YR 5/3) silty sand that contained small amounts of charcoal and burned clay. The silty sand, which was similar to disturbances caused 154 by large root or rodent activity, measured 70 cm east to west and extended beyond the cleared area to the east by 20 cm north to south (see Figure 51). Area B appears to be a root cast or rodent burrow; however, several pieces of charcoal and a small amount of burned clay were found adjacent to the apparent disturbance. Area B measured 14 cm north to south by 12 cm east to west. Additional brown silty sand-filled krotovina were observed between the three designated areas, although no burned clay or charcoal fragments were associated with these areas. Area C measured approximately 34 cm east to west by 14 cm north to south and consisted of a large concentration of strong brown (7.5YR 5/8) and yellowish red burned clay, surrounded by an intrusive brown (10YR 5/3) silty sand. The burned clay concentration of Area C gradually decreased in size as excavation of the feature continued. Figures 51 through 53 provide plan views of Feature 3 at 10-cm intervals from 160 cm bs to 190 cm bs, while the profile of the feature is illustrated in Figure 54. Areas A and B were very shallow and were no longer visible on the 170 cm bs plan view. The size of Area C was reduced considerably by 170 cm bs and consisted only of scattered burned clay fragments below 170 cm bs. Mussel shell fragments occurred between 170 and 180 cm bs and a concentration of more complete mussel shells approximately 5 cm in thickness was encountered at approximately 187 cm bs (see Figure 53). As in Feature 5 at site 41DL338, the shells were mostly complete and were concentrated in the western portion of the excavation area. Also like Feature 5 at site 41DL338, several of the recovered shells had been heat treated or charred (see Non-Artifactual Remains discussion below). No charcoal was associated with the shells, although a small burned clay stain was observed in the same area as the large concentration (see Figure 53). Two small pieces of charcoal were recovered from Area C and submitted for radiocarbon dating (Beta-129640). Analysis resulted in a date of 950 + 40 B.P. for this sample (see Radiocarbon Dating discussion below).

## Page 177

Material collected during the excavation of this feature is summarized in Table 50. Artifacts and ecofacts recovered from flotation samples are not included. However, the collected material consists of all artifacts and ecofacts, such as charcoal, shell, and burned clay, which would have been further fragmented or would have disintegrated during flotation. Much of the more complete shell identified between 170 and 190 cm bs is represented in this table. Several heat treated or charred specimens were also recovered from these excavation levels (see Non- Artifactual Remains discussion below), although the table illustrates that no charcoal was observed below 170 cm bs. While burned soil was observed below 170 cm bs, no concentrated chunks were available for collection. Figure 55 illustrates the strategy adopted in the sampling of this feature. An area measuring approximately 60 by 120 cm was excavated during the documentation of the feature. The entire area was divided in half from east to west and bisected Area C, the primary burned clay concentration (see Figure 55). The southern half of the feature was excavated first, leaving the north wall to be profiled (see below). Samples were collected from all three areas for flotation and are numbered 1 through 8 (see Figure 55). They measured approximately 40 by 15 cm and were 10 cm deep. Area C was divided into three sections, Areas A and B into two. Plan views Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites were drawn and the feature was photographed every 10 cm. Sample 1 was taken from the southern one-third of Area C, while Sample 2 was from the very southern edge of Area B and all of Area A. Samples 3, 5, 6, and 7 were similar sized samples collected from Area C between 160 and 180 cm bs (see Figure 55). Sample 8 was slightly larger in size (40 by 25 by 10 cm) so that the entire area under Area C was sampled together. In this way, recovery from the separate areas of the feature could be compared. Table 51 summarizes the recovery from the eight floated samples. Not surprisingly, Sample 3, collected from the center of Area C at a depth of 160 to 170 cm, produced the most burned clay, followed by Sample 5 directly to the north. Interestingly, these two samples produced the least mussel shell remains. Mussel shell clearly dominated the 180 to 190 cm bs level. These variations are illustrated when the density of shell, bone, and burned clay from the flotation samples are compared by level (Table 52). Of the three material types collected, mussel shell dominates all three 10-cm levels. However, when the density of burned clay is examined, level 160 to 170 cm bs produced considerably more than level 180 to 190 cm. The intervening level (170 to 180 cm bs) produced the fewest shell and burned clay remains but the only bone recovered from the eight flotation samples. ## Page 181

Unit 3 at 140 to 150cm bs. Elements from bobwhite, deer, and unidentifiable mammals were also identified. Although most faunal remains were from Unit 3, Units 6 and 12 produced seven and 10 specimens, respectively. Recovery from Units 5 and 7 and Feature 3 ranged from one to 4 specimens. Two bones each from small mammals and very small mammals that were recovered may not be associated with prehistoric subsistence. Three species of terrestrial gastropods (n=72) and eight species of fresh-water bivalves (n=560) were identified at site 41DL356 (see Appendix E). The species of mollusks recovered are similar to those that currently inhabit the area and suggest that the environment was similar to that of the present. Specifically, the gastropods indicate riparian woodlands with adjacent open, grassy areas and a climate of hot, dry summers and relatively mild winters and the bivalves indicate a stream or river environment. Much of the bivalve assemblage was likely utilized as a food resource as indicated by the presence of over 300 heat altered specimens, although it is possible that some of the untreated clams were eaten raw. Heat treatment indicates human activity because the bivalves were processed with hot water or steam as opposed to charring, which can occur naturally and will dissolve or significantly shrivel the body inside the shell. Four charred specimens (two each), which could indicate burning after the meat was removed, were recovered from Feature 3 and Unit 7. The charred and heat treated specimens identified from Feature 3 occurred between 160 and 170 cm bs, with most from 180 to 190 cm bs. Other heated specimens were identified from Unit 6 at 110 to 120 cm bs and Unit 7 at 190 to 230 cm bs. Most from the latter test unit occurred at 220 to 230 cm bs. The distribution of both treated and untreated shells indicates utilization of this site over a long period of time. The overwhelming majority of the specimens were immature. This suggests that a constant collecting pressure was exerted on the local bivalve populations. No evidence was found for the use of shells for tools or ornaments. 163 Chapter 5: Results of Evaluations of Eight Sites In addition, one otolith was recovered from Trench 17 at 50 to 65 cm bs (see Appendix D). It was identified as being from a freshwater drum (Aplodinotus _grunniens) that measured approximately 30 cm in length. The bone could have arrived at the site via flood water transport, raptor transport, or human activity. The mussel shells recovered from the site probably aided in the otolith’s preservation by increasing or maintaining the soil alkalinity of the surrounding soil. Therefore, it is probable that the bone was deposited at roughly the same time as the shells. Special Analyses Flotation Analyses In addition to the soil samples collected in each of the four features identified at site 41DL356, a column sample of .008 m? soil samples for flotation was collected from Unit 6. The samples were collected from 90 cm bs to the base of the unit excavations at 210 cm bs. Table 53 summarizes the recovery from these samples. Generally, the recovery from the flotation samples resembles that of the soil screened through 6.4-mm (%4-in) mesh in the field. The peak in mussel shell in the 150 to 170-cm level is likely due to the proximity of the column sample to the shell lens identified as Feature 4. As in the unit excavations, the small amount of bone, charcoal, and burned clay from the flotation samples was from the levels above the shell concentration. Radiocarbon Dating Five samples from various levels of the West Fork soil were submitted for radiocarbon analysis (see Appendix G). One sample was taken from Unit 6 at the top of the West Fork soil (Beta-12938) and another was taken from the same unit at the bottom of the West Fork soil (Beta-129639). These samples produced at range of 2,560 + 50 B.P. to 1,390 + 50 B.P. for the paleosol. These dates are consistent for the

Therefore, it is recommended that site 41DL356 be considered ineligible for inclusion in the NRHP.
