## Page 183

CHAPTER 6 SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS by Duane E. Peter, Johnna L. Buysee, and Angela L. Tiné Rechannelization of the Trinity River near Interstate 45 has been planned as part of a project by the City of Dallas and the USACE, Fort Worth District, to extend the Dallas Floodway south along the Trinity River flood plain between the Corinth Street Viaduct and Loop 12. Seven prehistoric archeological sites had been identified previously within the projected APE of rechannelization efforts between the Central Wastewater Treatment Plant and approximately 200 m east of Interstate 45 along the south bank of the Trinity River. The threat of destruction of these sites from rechannelization activities prompted the current projects. Between September 2 and October 2, 1998, and January 26 and March 5, 1999, test excavations were conducted at these sites (41DL318, 41DL319, 41DL357, 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356) to determine their potential to yield data significant to an understanding of the prehistory of the upper Trinity River basin and, thus, their eligibility for inclusion in the National Register of Historic Places. The first series of investigations involved sites 41DL318, 41DL319, and 41DL357. These sites are located east of Interstate 45. Mussel shell, bone, burned rock, and lithic artifacts (41DL319 and 41DL357 only) had been exposed in the cutbank on the south side of the Trinity River. The material appeared to originate in what is a dark organic horizon, presently identified as the 165 West Fork paleosol, approximately 1 to 2 m below ground surface. The testing program at these sites was initiated with mechanical trenching along the existing cutbank and was followed by hand excavation of test units in areas with the greatest potential for producing cultural material. Prehistoric deposits were identified at varying depths within the West Fork paleosol. The continuous scatter of cultural material between sites 41DL319 and 41DL357 prompted the combination of these two sites. The primary evidence of prehistoric occupation at sites 41DL318 and 41DL319/357 was the presence of mussel shell fragments, occasionally associated with areas of burned clay or fragments of animal bone. Analysis of the shells indicated probable human consumption of freshwater bivalves because of the presence of heat treated specimens. Vertebrate faunal analysis suggests a diet based on terrestrial mammals, primarily deer, supplemented with birds and fish. Features of burned clay and/or soil were identified at both sites. Only a small amount of artifacts were found in association with these features. Of the sites investigated by this project, these two were the only to produce lithic artifacts. A few small chipped stone fragments were recovered from a feature at site 41DL318, and a sparse deposit of lithic artifacts was retrieved from site 41DL319/357. Radiocarbon dates indicate that at least one occupation at site


![Image 1 - Page 183](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p183_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 183*


---

## Page 184

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas 41DL318 occurred ca. 1,000 + 50 B.P., which corresponds to the Late Prehistoric I period in the region. Utilization of 41DL319/357 dates slightly earlier to between 3,050 + 60 B.P. and 1,420 + 60 B.P., or the Late Archaic period. Sites 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356, located west of Interstate 45 at the northeast edge of the Central Wastewater Treatment Plant, were identified during activities associated with the construction of an effluent outflow channel. The sites were recorded as concentrations of mussel shells and small amounts of bone and burned rock exposed in the bank of the outflow channel. Testing of these sites also consisted of mechanical trenching followed by handexcavation of test units in specific areas. Trenching at these sites identified the settling ponds used during the early years of the treatment plant, and construction appears to have heavily impacted these resources. Site 41DL355 was found to contain no intact deposits. The mussel shell identified at the site was determined to be part of fill deposits. Sites 41DL337, 41DL338, and 41DL356 had also been impacted by settling pond and outflow channel construction, although limited intact deposits were identified at these sites. These deposits consisted primarily of mussel shells and mussel shell fragments, with occasional bone or burned clay fragments. Recovery at site 41DL337 was sparse and consisted almost entirely of mussel shell fragments scattered throughout the West Fork soil. No concentration of shell or burned clay was observed at this site. The largest shell concentrations in the entire study area were observed at sites 41DL338 and 41DL356. Although peak periods of site utilization are suggested by extremely large deposits, overall sizable deposits are present to a considerable depth and indicate intensive, albeit short-term, use of these sites over a long period. Analysis of the shells recovered from sites 41DL337, 41DL338 and 41DL356 indicates human consumption of fresh-water bivalves as evidenced by the presence of heat-treated shells and shell fragments. Considerably more evidence for exploitation of aquatic vertebrates 166 for food (e.g., fish and turtle) exists at these sites than at sites 41DL318 and 41DL319/357. Burned clay features are the only other cultural manifestations represented (site 41DL356 only), for no artifacts were recovered even though flotation of samples was conducted to ensure that micro-flakes would be recovered. Radiocarbon dates generated from samples from sites 41DL338 and 41DL356_ indicated Late Prehistoric II (480 + 40 B.P.) and Late Archaic 2,460 + 50 B.P. to 1,390 +50 B.P.) occupations, respectively. The evaluation of these sites concerning their eligibility for inclusion in the NRHP is dependent on several factors: (1) the state of knowledge concerning the prehistoric use of the region, particularly the use of the Trinity River flood plain; (2) the contextual integrity of the deposits; and (3) whether the data recovered will enhance our understanding of the prehistoric occupation of the upper Trinity River drainage during the late Holocene period. The state of scientific knowledge concerning the prehistoric use of the landforms within the upper Trinity River basin is very limited for the late Holocene period, Although this state of knowledge is partially limited by the lack of intensive data recovery investigations and regional syntheses, investigators have been stymied by the challenges of interpreting such low density sites within flood plain deposits. Previous investigations along both the West Fork and the Elm Fork of the Trinity River (Ellis 1998; Ferring 1990; Ferring and Byers 1996; Ferring and Yates 1997; Hunt and Peter 1997a; Peter and McGregor 1998; Peter et al. 1987; Peter et al. 1998) have revealed that prehistoric use of the flood plain frequently resulted in archeological deposits not unlike those revealed at sites 41DL318, 41DL319/357, 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356. Sites of the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods found within the flood plain frequently exhibit very sparse scatters of faunal elements and burned rock with even fewer lithic tools or pieces of debitage. Since detailed examination of terrace site contexts has been limited to only major reservoir projects (Ferring and Yates 1997,


![Image 1 - Page 184](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p184_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 184*


---

## Page 185

1998; Peter et al. 1988), an understanding of the relationship of the short-term, but frequent, use of the Trinity River flood plain to the overall settlement/subsistence pattern during the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods is not clear. Of course, preservation of such flood plain sites and/or the data recovery efforts to mitigate their loss is dependent upon whether these sites retain contextual integrity and will provide a data base essential to understanding the prehistoric use of the upper Trinity River basin. The matter of contextual integrity is of primary importance in evaluating sites 41DL318, 41DL319/357, 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356. Of the sites below the I-45 bridge (41DL318 and 41DL319/357), site 41DL319/357 exhibited the most comprehensive artifact assemblage and multiple features. Although it is tempting to recognize multiple occupational episodes within the site deposits, the evidence of the vertisolic cracks within the West Fork paleosol together with the presence of modern debris at 2.5 m below the flood plain surface raises serious questions concerning the vertical integrity of the deposits. Examination of the vertical distribution of the cultural material (see Figure 14) reveals that lithic artifacts are found in a 60- 80 cm segment of the profiles while burned clay and mussel shell are found throughout a 1.5-2.0- m deep segment. Whether all of the burned clay and mussel shell represent actual refuse from human occupation has not been demonstrated. Although four radiocarbon dates were obtained from site 41DL319/357 (Table 54), only one sample from Feature 2 was obtained from charcoal. This sample clearly indicates a short- term use of the flood plain at ca. 180 B.C. The remaining radiocarbon dates indicate that the A- horizon of the West Fork paleosol was accumulating between ca. 900 B.C. and A.D. 640. Mussel shell and burned clay are represented throughout this period. The vertisolic nature of the sediments raises serious questions concerning the interpretation of the human use of this landform. It is clear that the area was used for a short term during the Late Archaic period; the frequency of the reoccurring 167 Chapter 6: Summary and Recommendations use of this landform is largely a matter of speculation. The situation at site 41DL318 is somewhat different, for the distribution of bone, charcoal, and mussel shell extends from the middle of the A-horizon overlying the West Fork paleosol to 150 cm below the surface of the West Fork paleosol (see Figure 8). Since Feature 3 yielded a calibrated radiocarbon date of A.D. 1020 (see Table 54), one might interpret that the mussel shell, bone, and charcoal found in the overlying A-horizon might reflect a much later occupation. Unfortunately, such an interpretation is based upon the assumption that these materials are the manifestation of human occupation. Since such remains may have been redeposited at this location or are the result of natural phenomena, the use of this particular location for human habitation, however short-lived, may have been restricted to the upper meter of the West Fork paleosol. Although similar problems in interpreting whether or not the mussel shell and bone represent actual human occupation are relevant at sites 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356, contextual integrity is further impacted by the construction and use of the Central Wastewater Treatment Plant. All four sites, perhaps one site originally, have been impacted by the construction of settling ponds and the outflow channel. Nevertheless, segments of the sites appear to be intact. As with the othér sites, the vertical distribution of bone and shell is significantly greater than that of identifiable features. Two radiocarbon dates, both from charcoal samples, reveal that human occupation is represented between ca. A.D. 1000 and A.D. 1430. Unfortunately, the disturbed nature of the site or sites and the lack of recognizable stratigraphy within the A-horizon of the West Fork paleosol preclude the reconstruction of occupational living surfaces. Given that most of the cultural deposits occur in the soil horizon recognized as the West Fork paleosol, it could be assumed that such sites

## Page 187

provide information critical to an understanding of regional climatic and hydrographic changes and the resulting adjustments of prehistoric populations. Clearly, flood plain sites can provide data important to an understanding of climatic changes and resulting variations in flood plain deposition and soil formation. One question of particular interest to regional researchers is the significance of the West Fork paleosol as a regional marker horizon. Does the West Fork paleosol form throughout the upper Trinity River basin at roughly the same time or is there significant temporal variation due to spatial variation in depositional environments and geomorphic controls on deposition? The answer to this question has been elusive, for investigations have focused almost totally on the meanderbelt facies of the soil and the dating of the horizon has been largely dependent on the dating of soil humates. As noted by Ferring (1990:48), the West Fork paleosol formed on flood plains between 4,500 B.P. and the present. This soil formed in the gradually aggrading Pilot Point alluvium. A facies of the West Fork paleosol formed along meanderbelts as the rate of lateral channel migration slowed between 2,000 and 500 B.P. The West Fork paleosol has formed in late Holocene alluvium that is inset against older alluvium. The West Fork paleosol is frequently found buried by recent alluvium. Away from the modern meanderbelts, the West Fork soil is the surface soil, which has formed on flood basin clays and silts and has an overthickened A horizon approximately 2 m thick. Geoarcheological investigations of this latter facies have been limited in focus. At first glance, the radiocarbon dates obtained from the investigations of sites 41DL318, 41DL319/357, 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356 and the distribution of cultural refuse provide data essential to an understanding of the changing environment and the impact on the use of the landscape by prehistoric hunters and gatherers. An examination of Table 54 and Figures 57 and 58 reveals that both Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric populations were using the 169 Chapter 6: Summary and Recommendations Trinity River flood plain during the accumulation of the West Fork soil. However, a closer examination of the humate and charcoal derived dates in relation to the West Fork soil stratigraphy reveals a rather chaotic sequence of dates relative to the formation of the West Fork soil. Examination of Figures 57 and 58 reveals that the surface of the West Fork soil may date as early as 2,000 B.P. or as late as A.D. 1430. Given the close proximity of the seven sites and the likely similarities in depositional environment, such variation is not likely. Of course, the disturbances to site 41D1356 and the likely removal of the upper part of the West Fork soil in the vicinity of Trench 16 at site 41DL319/357 for the construction of Miller’s Bridge have had an impact on such results. However, the sequence of dates in THT 36 at site 41DL319/357 (see Figure 58) and the relative consistency of the charcoal-derived dates (see Table 54) indicate that there is a greater problem. This problem reflects an almost total reliance on soil humate-derived dates for understanding the timing of the West Fork soil formation. As Wang et al. (1996:287) have noted, ““C dating of soil organic matter could overestimate the true age of the burial by as much as the steady-state age of the soil or soil horizon which is a function of climate and other factors.” Nevertheless, the dating of soil humates has been consistently used by regional researchers when charcoal from cultural features is not available. This reliance on humate- derived dates, however, raises questions concerning the actual temporal framework for the West Fork soil and the associated human occupations. As Peter et al. (1998:46-47) have noted previously, the dates for the West Fork paleosol largely fall within the range (1,985 yr B.P. to 528 yr B.P.) defined by Ferring (1990:Table 11) at seven different locations in the upper Trinity drainage. Studies at the River Bend site (41TR68) yielded uncorrected dates of 750 + 50 B.P. (Beta-22487) and 620 + 100 B.P. (Beta- 22487) from Features 2 and 3, respectively (Peter et al. 1987:17-18). These dates are from charcoal samples collected from features located

## Page 190

An Evaluation of Sites within the Proposed Dallas Floodway Extension Project, Dallas County, Texas at the surface of the West Fork paleosol. More recent work along the West Fork has yielded dates of 1,730 + 50 B.P. (Beta-131368; cal A.D. 330) at site 41TR174 (Burson et al. 2000) and 1,330 + 60 B.P.; cal A.D. 675) at site 41TR162 (Skinner et al. 1999). The former date is from a context 90 cm below the surface of the West Fork while the latter is from a context 35 cm below the surface of the West Fork. Both dates were derived from charcoal samples within the West Fork flood plain. Both samples are within the A horizon of the West Fork soil. The investigations conducted by the Texas Department of Transportation at site 41TR167 yielded a date of 1,130 + B.P. (Beta-108117; cal A.D. 779-1010 [95% confidence interval]). The sample was charcoal from a hearth located at the top of the BC horizon approximately 1 m below the surface of the West Fork paleosol (Ellis 1998:Figure 2A). Elsewhere along the Elm Fork of the Trinity River, Hunt et al. (2000) have submitted soil humate samples for analysis that yielded dates ranging from 2,130 + 70 B.P. (Beta-122991; cal B.C 165) to 1,660 + 60 B.P. (Beta-122994; cal A.D. 410). A single charcoal sample yielded a date of 670 + 90 B.P. (Beta- 122993; cal A.D. 1300). Farther afield, investigations at Richland-Chambers Creek revealed that the upper portion of the Navarro paleosol (horizon VA1b) dates from A.D. 670 to A.D, 1820. Although all dates are derived from charcoal or nut shell samples, one of the primary authors remains skeptical about what was being dated in relation to Features 201 and 203. McGregor (personal communication 2000) feels that the charcoal samples may have been taken from intrusive burned tree roots. The utility of the West Fork paleosol as a regional horizon marker remains to be fully demonstrated even though most researchers in Northcentral Texas are inclined to view it as such. At the present, however, care should be taken in extrapolating anything more than a Late Holocene date between A.D. 0 and A.D. 1300. The formation of the West Fork paleosol is likely linked to regional climatic changes and associated thresholds of overbank deposition; however, local depositional factors—sediment supply, proximity to the river channel, width of the river 172 valley, and so on—had a major impact on its formation and the age of its eventual burial. From the investigations conducted during the past decade, it is clear that evidence of the prehistoric inhabitants of the upper Trinity River basin is represented in the West Fork soil. The significance of these sites and their data potential have become a matter of concern to the archeological community (Burson et al. 2000; Ellis 1998). These investigations have revealed that these flood plain sites usually yield a very limited chipped stone artifact assemblage with varying amounts of burned rock, mammal bone, and mussel shell (Peter and McGregor 1988; Peter et al. 1987; Peter et al. 1988; Skinner et al. 1999). Other than the attempts by Brown et al. (1998) to extract climatic data through the examination of oxygen isotopes, analyses of the bivalves and pelecypods and the sedimentary geology have been the only means used to present indications of the local prehistoric environment during the Late Holocene. Although Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric occupational sites have been investigated within the upper Trinity River flood plain, the role of the flood plain sites within the overall settlement/subsistence pattern and the nature of the changing adaptations during these time periods is unclear. The lack of good stratified sites with good contextual integrity and features with datable charcoal has contributed to this situation. Sites, such as 41DL318, 41DL319/357, 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356, provide very limited data that are relevant to an understanding of the changing Late Holocene environment and the associated prehistoric adaptations. If we are to understand the Late Holocene adaptations, researchers must focus on sites that provide discrete components with features with datable charcoal. The continued reliance on soil humate-derived dates will contribute to further confusion rather than an understanding of the temporal framework of the cultural adaptations. It is clear that prehistoric hunters and gatherers were using the flood plain environment frequently during the Late ‘Holocene; however, the examination of sites similar to those reported here will not


![Image 1 - Page 190](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p190_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 190*


---

## Page 191

contribute to a better understanding of the Late Holocene adaptations. Only sites like East First Street Bridge (41TR168), possibly site 41TR174, and the River Bend site (41TR168) offer the data sets necessary for gaining a better understanding of the local prehistory. Another limiting factor to our understanding of the adaptations represented by these low-density flood plain sites is traditional archeological methodology. Flood plain sites, because of their potential depth, are frequently sampled minimally through the placement of a limited number of backhoe trenches and an even more limited number of controlled excavation units. This telephone booth approach to such sites provides a very narrow window into often expansive flood plain sites. Intrasite variability is consequently very poorly understood, for the patchy nature of the horizontal distribution of artifacts and features is very poorly sampled. It is clear that the monitoring and expeditious recording of features and artifact scatters during the mechanical excavation of flood plain deposits would provide a much better perspective of the content and structure of these flood plain sites. Based on the testing operations it has been determined that the above sites have little research potential. Settling ponds and construction of the effluent outflow channel in 173 Chapter 6: Summary and Recommendations the early 1990s have destroyed any intact subsurface deposits at site 41D1355 that may have been present. While intact subsurface deposits were identified at the remaining five sites, the remains observed during testing were either extremely sparse or lacked artifactual remains. Only sites 41DL318 and 41DL319/357 produced lithic artifacts. Burned clay and/or mussel shell features were identified at sites 41DL318, 41DL319/357, 41DL338, and 41DL356; however, none of the features were associated with artifactual remains. In addition, disturbances associated with the early use of the Central Waste Water Treatment Plant were found to be extensive in the areas of sites 41DL318, 41DL338, and 41DL356, and have very likely destroyed portions of these sites. Although a significant amount of information was gathered regarding the formation of the West Fork soil and its relationship to the prehistoric occupation of the area, very little cultural material was present at any site. While the information gathered from the current investigation will add to our understanding of prehistoric occupation along the Trinity River, it is unlikely that further investigations at these sites would significantly add to our knowledge. Therefore, it is recommended that sites 41DL318, 41DL319/357, 41DL337, 41DL338, 41DL355, and 41DL356 be considered ineligible for inclusion in the NRHP.

## Page 277

APPENDIX C FAUNAL ANALYSIS by LeeAnna Schniebs ArchaeoFaunas Sanger, Texas and Maynard B. Cliff Geo-Marine, Inc.

## Page 278


![Image 1 - Page 278](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p278_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 278*


---

## Page 279

Investigations prior to the extension of the Dallas Floodway south along the Trinity River yielded 223 faunal specimens from four sites in Dallas County, Texas. Total weight of the entire assemblage is 296.83 grams. Three sites are associated with Late Archaic or Late Prehistoric occupations, and one is in a derived context. Faunal material was recovered from trenches and hand-excavated units and the modern ground surface at one site. The following sections - discuss the methods employed in the faunal analysis, results of taxonomic identification and quantification, and distribution of these remains. METHODOLOGY All prehistoric vertebrate remains were inventoried, using Excel 5.0 for Windows to generate the data base. An Ohaus digital scale, Model CT600-S, was used to record bone weight. Provenience information was recorded during analysis. All fragments recovered were analyzed by the author, using comparative collections on loan from, or housed at, the Institute of Applied Sciences, Zooarchaeology Lab, University of North Texas, Denton. Occasional supplements were required including conventional osteological keys such as Olsen (1964), Gilbert (1980), and Schmid (1972). Identifications were made to the most specific category possible depending on the condition of the bone and available comparative material. Only positive identifications resulted in the assignment of elements to genus or species. Standard zooarchaeological methods were used. The animal bones were cleaned, counted, and bagged in the Geo- Marine, Inc., lab in Plano, Texas, then submitted for identification and quantification. Both unidentifiable and identifiable pieces were analyzed in a similar fashion. That is, the same attributes were recorded: taxon; element and portion of that element; anatomical location of the element; age of individual; and condition of the bone, including any notes on taphonomy, burning or breakage patterns, gnawing, and cultural modification, if applicable. The assemblage is quantified on the basis of the number of identified specimens per taxon (NISP) and minimum number of individuals (MNI) for identified elements. MNI estimates were calculated according to the most frequently occurring element, which was based on symmetry and element portion (Munzel 1986). In the mammalian class, teeth were used whenever possible. Teeth retained in sockets were recorded but not counted or weighed. Loose teeth (those not in the socket) were counted and weighed as separate elements. This allowed for MNI calculations without elevating specimen counts or skewing the data. Other attributes used in defining MNI included age (based on tooth eruption and occlusal wear and/or epiphyseal fusion) and the relative sizes of otherwise similar specimens in the comparative collection. In some cases, complete long bones and proximal or distal ends were considered. The faunal tables in this appendix are standard species lists with the number of occurrences for each animal. Those specimens regarded as unidentifiable (i.e., those coded to only class or order) have been consolidated into a few general categories, Elements of nondiagnostic skeletal value (i.e., ribs, vertebrae, and long bone shafts; Olsen 1964), have been coded in an indeterminate category by class and/or size range. Specifically, specimens counted as “very small mammal” are from mouse-size animals. Those classified as “small mammal” are from gopher or rabbit-size mammals. The “medium mammal” category includes dog or sheep-size animals. “Large mammal” refers to deer size, whereas “very large mammal” is bison size. “Indeterminate vertebrate” includes the bones of uncertain class. Recording these specimens in a size category enables the most precise level of observation the specimen allows. In small samples, taking note of weight and the size categories of nondiagnostic elements broadens the function of the bone assemblage. However, percentages referred to in this report are calculated by number of bones (NISP) rather than weight. Weight of specimens by bag number are listed in the data sheets. ANALYSIS RESULTS The following section describes the animals identified in the faunal assemblages from the four sites. Taxonomic classes identified include fish, reptile, bird (aves), and mammal (rodentia, canidae, and artiodactyla). Remains from unidentifiable mammals of all sizes and indeterminate vertebrates are also included in the project assemblage. No specimens were culturally modified. Number of identified specimens (NISP) and minimum number of individuals (MNI) for each taxon are summarized in Table C-1, as are weights for each taxon and percentages of site assemblage. Composition of anatomical elements can be found in Table C-2.

## Page 283

Assemblage Composition Class Osteichthyes Order Lepisosteiformes, Family Lepisosteidae: Six gar (Lepisosteus sp.) scales were recovered from 190-200 cm bs (Level 20) of Unit 8 at 41DL338. The specimens were not burned. Gars are cigar-shaped predatory fish with thick ganoid (diamond-shaped) scales and beaklike jaws with sharp pointed teeth. They are known to frequent large streams, rivers, and shallow weedy lakes, where they spawn in spring. They can use atmospheric oxygen and may bask on the surface of the water (Collins 1959). Four species of gar were present, but specific identification was not possible. Order Siluriformes, Family Ictaluridae: One catfish Uictalurus sp.) bone was recovered from a flotation sample from 180-200 cm bs in Unit 8 at 41DL338. The piece was not burned. Catfish are widely distributed throughout the region in various types of bodies of water. They have a poison gland at the base of the pectoral spines that can cause a painful wound, but it does not affect the edibility of the fish (Collins 1959). Order indeterminate: Two very small fragments from unidentifiable medium-sized bony fish were present in the 41DL338 faunal collection. A rib fragment came from a flotation sample taken from Feature 5 (160-170 cm bs) in Unit 8, while a spinous process fragment was recovered from 190-200 cm bs (Level 20) of the same unit. The pieces were not burned. The recovery of fish remains is not surprising because the sites are located so close to the Trinity River. Undoubtedly the inhabitants of the sites utilized the resources of the river, including fish. Class Reptilia Order Testudinata, Family Indeterminate: Shell fragments from unidentifiable turtle were recovered from two sites, One specimen came from a flotation sample taken from 180-200 cm bs in Unit 8 at 41DL338, while 28 specimens were recovered from four different units at 41DL356, specifically, Unit 3, 140-150 cm bs (Level 15, n=25); Unit 5, 110-120 cm bs (Level 12, n=1); Unit 6, 200-210 cm bs (Level 21, n=1); and Unit 7, 180-190 cm bs (Level 19, n=1). The fragments were not burned, but 27 (i.e., those from Units 3, 5, and 7) were abraded. Fragmentation prevented specific identification of the turtle remains. Class Aves Order Galliformes, Family Phasianidae: Northern bobwhite (Colinus virginianus) was represented by one bone fragment recovered 170 cm bs in Unit 12 at 41DL356. The specimen was not burned. The bobwhite is found throughout the eastern United States and is abundant in brush, abandoned fields, and open pine-lands but avoids deep forests (Robbins et al. 1983). Two very.small long bone fragments from an unidentifiable medium-size bird were also included in the faunal assemblage. They were recovered from Unit 1, 80-90 cm bs (Level 2), at 41DL318. The specimens were not burned. Class Mammalia Order Rodentia, Family Geomyidae: Plains pocket gopher (Geomys bursarius) was represented by one tooth fragment recovered from Unit 4, 220-230 cm bs (Level 6), at 41DL319/357. In general, pocket gophers reportedly prefer grassland, alfalfa fields, pastures, roadsides, and railroad rights-of-way (Burt and Grossenheider 1980). In Texas, Geomys bursarius reaches its easternmost limit in the Brazos River watershed in McLennan County, and the Trinity River watershed in Dallas, Tarrant, and Denton counties (Schmidly 1983:143, Map 28). It occupies the sandy, alluvial deposits along each side of these rivers and their major tributaries. This specimen was the only evidence of pocket gopher recovered from the Dallas Floodway sites. C-7


![Image 1 - Page 283](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p283_img1.png)

## Page 284

Order Rodentia, Family Myocastoridae: Nutria (Myocastor coypus) was represented by a mandible with teeth recovered from Backhoe Trench 13 (depth unknown) at 41DL319/357. The specimen was not burned but was etched. This aquatic animal prefers marshes, swamps, ponds, and lakes, and makes shallow burrows in banks with an enlarged nesting chamber (Burt and Grossenheider 1980). Of South American origin, the nutria was originally introduced into southern Louisiana in the vicinity of Aver Island in 1938. In 1946, nutria were released in Anderson and Houston counties, south of Dallas along the Trinity River. Since that time, they have spread throughout all of eastern and central Texas (Schmidly 1983:223, Map225). This specimen could not be associated with prehistoric subsistence in the Trinity River flood plain. Order Carnivora, Family Canidae: One specimen recorded as Canis sp. represents coyote or domestic dog. This lower incisor was recovered from 250-260 cm bs (Level 6), in Unit 6 at 41DL319/357. It is severely worn and, therefore, indicates an older individual. The project area is included in the range of the coyote (Canis latrans). This animal preferred prairies, open woodlands, brushy, or boulder-strewn areas (Burt and Grossenheider 1980) and is hunted for its pelt as well as because it is a nuisance. The domestic dog (Canis familiaris) had arrived by Archaic times (Newcomb 1993) and is often found in prehistoric contexts. Order Artiodactyla, Family Cervidae: Deer (Odocoileus sp.) was represented at three sites. One tooth fragment was recovered at 190-200 cm bs in Trench | at 41DL318. Five tooth fragments were recovered from three different units at 41DL319/357, specifically, Unit 3, 240-150 cm bs (Level 9, n=2); Unit 4, 200-210 cm bs (Level 4, n=1) and 220- 230 cm bs (Level 6, n=1); and Unit 5, 280-290 cm bs (Level 11, n=1). Three tooth fragments were recovered from Unit 12, 210-220 cm bs (Level 22), at 41DL356. None of these specimens were burned. Whitetailed deer (Odocoileus virginianus) is the only species that currently occupies the project area. It is found in forests, swamps, and open brushy areas nearby (Burt and Grossenheider 1980). Prehistorically, other species may have been present, but the elements found in this assemblage are from smaller individuals. Whitetailed deer are known for their small size compared to the larger Mule Deer of the western United States. Medium artiodactyl were represented by 12 fragments recovered from site 41DL319/357. Two specimens were recovered from Unit 4, 180-190 cm bs (Level 2). One specimen came from the backfill of Trench 11 (depth unknown). The remaining nine specimens came from the cutbank adjacent to the northern end of Trench 11, between 40 and 50 cm bs. These pieces were not burned. The sample consisted entirely of lower limb bones (i.e., “non-meat items”), These are most likely the remains of deer rather than pronghorn (Antilocapra americana). Both are similar in size, but pronghorn antelope are found in open prairies and sagebrush plains well outside of the project area (Burt and Grossenheider 1980). The two are osteologically similar, but specific identification was not recorded because of fragmentation. In addition to these, medium artiodactyls were probably represented in the unidentifiable large mammal category. Large mammal remains were recovered from all four sites. Large artiodactyl was represented by five fragments recovered from the cutbank adjacent to the northern end of Trench 11, at 41DL319/357, between 40 and 50 cm bs. The elements were unfused, indicating an immature individual. They were not burned. These are most likely the remains of bison (Bison sp.) rather than domestic cow. The single specimen recorded as very large mammal from the same context may be bison remains as well. Site 41DL3 19/357 was the only site to yield elements from animals of this size. Indeterminate Two sites yielded remains from indeterminate vertebrates. These bone fragments were unidentifiable even at the class level. Fifteen specimens were recovered from five units at site 41DL319/357, representing 13 percent of the vertebrate faunal sample from that site. The contexts for this material includes: Unit 4, 210-220 cm bs (Level 5, n=1), 240-250 cm bs (Level 8, n=2), and 250-260 cm bs (Level 9, n=1); Unit 5, 280-290 cm bs (Level 11, n=1); Unit 7, 220-230 cm bs (n=2); Feature 2, 370-385 cm bs (n=4); and Feature 3, 130-140 cm bs (n=4). Six specimens were burned, and 10 came from flotation samples. Site 41DL356 yielded four fragments from two different units, including Unit 6, 130- 150 cm bs (n=3) and Unit 7, 240-250 (Level 25, n=1). The three pieces from Unit 6 were recovered from a flotation sample. None of the pieces were burned. A large portion of the project sample was not identifiable beyond size and class categories. Remains from mammals of all sizes were recovered from all sites and were dominated by large mammal bone fragments.


![Image 1 - Page 284](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p284_img1.png)

## Page 285

In general, the faunal samples were highly fragmented. This explains the low rate of identification. Exfoliation occurred most frequently (Table C-3). Six specimens were carnivore-gnawed, three were rodent-gnawed, and eight were spirally fractured (Table C-4). Spiral fractures are the result of impact, such as striking with a hammerstone or breaking on an anvil. It is a common, expedient technique used in tool manufacture, bone processing, and refuse disposal. Usually associated with large mammal long bones, spiral fractures can also be due to trampling, carnivore gnawing, or any other severe impacts not necessarily associated with human activity. The angular fracturing on most of the bone fragments suggests that they were broken while dry, possibly during the process of marrow extraction for bone grease, which involves the bones being broken into small pieces and boiled in water. The floating fat is then skimmed from the top of the pot. The substance is used for frying and other culinary purposes. This practice has been Assemblage Condition well documented, and is a method used by many different cultures (Leechman 1951). Distribution The following section is organized by site and discusses recovery from each context. This information is summarized in Table C-6. 41DL318 Forty-eight faunal specimens were recovered from two units and four different trenches at this short-term occupation site on the southwest bank of the Trinity River. The sample is dominated by large mammal remains (n=29, 61 percent). These fragments were dispersed throughout the site, recovered from Unit i and the four trenches. One deer tooth fragment from Trench 1 is the only identifiable specimen in the entire sample. Three levels in Unit 2 yielded the 15 indeterminate mammal remains. The only bone from a small mammal in the sample was recovered from Trench 1, 120 cm bs, and may not be associated with prehistoric subsistence. 41DL319/357 One hundred and twelve faunal specimens were recovered from this site on the southwest bank of the Trinity River. This sample is also dominated by large mammal (n=59, 53 percent), but includes pocket gopher, nutria, canid, deer, and medium and large artiodactyl remains. Faunal specimens were recovered in small quantities from numerous units and trenches, with totals ranging from one to 13. The exception is the cutbank adjacent to Trench 11 which yielded 55 specimens, consisting of large mammal (n=40), very large mammal (n=1), medium artiodactyl (n=9), and large artiodactyl (m=5). However, these fragments were recovered 40-50 cm bs, well above the levels of prehistoric occupation. The three features (Features 1, 2, and 3) yielded a combined total of only nine specimens, consisting of indeterminate vertebrate and unidentifiable mammal bone fragments. 41DL338 Only 14 faunal specimens were recovered from this site located along the cutbank on the west side of the outflow channel of the Central Wastewater Treatment Plant. The sample consists of aquatic animal remains (unidentifiable fish, gar, catfish, and turtle), one unidentifiable mammal bone, and three bones from large mammals. Most of the specimens came from 190-200 cm bs in Unit 8 (n=11). The remainder were from 160-170 cm bs (n=1) and 170- 180 cm bs (n=2) of the same unit. This sample can be considered debris from dietary supplements. C-10

## Page 290

41DL356 Forty-nine faunal specimens were recovered from this site on the east bank of the outflow channel. The sample was dominated by unidentifiable turtle shell fragments (n=28, 57 percent). Most of these were recovered from 140-150 cm bs in Unit 3. The remainder of the collection consisted of bobwhite, deer, and unidentifiable mammal bones of all sizes. Units 5, 6, 7, and 12 yielded totals ranging from two to 10 specimens. Feature 3 produced only one element of a very small mammal from 170-180 cm bs. The two bones from very small mammals and the two from small mammals may not be associated with the prehistoric occupation. SUMMARY The faunal samples from sites 41DL318, 41DL319/357, 41DL338, and 41DL356 are evidence of dietary refuse, and reflect a reliance on large game animals, supplemented by fish, turtle, and occasionally birds and small mammals. The canine may reflect possible domestication. Hunting episodes are indicated by the presence of medium and large artiodactyl remains. Taxonomic composition is typical of prehistoric subsistence strategies. C-14

## Page 293

APPENDIX D OTOLITH ANALYSIS by W. L. McClure, PE Engineering and Environmental Consultant Houston, Texas


![Image 1 - Page 293](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p293_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 293*

## Page 294


![Image 1 - Page 294](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p294_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 294*


---

## Page 295

Otoliths from two fish were recovered during test excavatons on the Trinity Floodway sites and were submitted for identification and analysis. Both sagitta otoliths are from freshwater drum (Aplodinoutus grunniens). The specimen from site 41DL319/357, Unit 3, 270-280 cm bs, is from a fish about 60 cm in length. The otolith from site 41DL356, Trench 17, 50-65 cm bs, is from a fish about 30 cm in length. The freshwater drum ranges from Canada to Mexico and is common in Dallas County today (Hubbs 1982), It is the only freshwater species of a family whose other members have marine and estuarine habitat requirements. The freshwater drum is primarily found in large rivers and lakes (McClane 1978). Class Osteoichthyes Infra-class Teleostei Order Perciformes Family Sciaenidae Genus Aplodinotus Species grunniens These fish would have been available in the Trinity River and some of its tributaries at the time of occupation of the prehistoric sites. The otoliths could have arrived at the position of recovery by floodwater transport, raptor transport, or by human activity. Otoliths and other bones of this fish are frequently found in acheologicial sites and within nest debris of the bald eagle (Haliaeetus leucocephalus). Otoliths are subject to destruction in soils that are weakly acidic. The presence of shells of freshwater mussels probably aided in maintaining an alkaline soil condition and preserving these otoliths. Thus, it is concluded that the otoliths were deposited concurrently with the shells. D-3

## Page 299

APPENDIX E MOLLUSCAN ANALYSIS by Richard W. Fullington, Ph.D. Worldwide Natural Sciences, Inc. Dallas, Texas


![Image 1 - Page 299](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p299_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 299*


---

## Page 300


![Image 1 - Page 300](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p300_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 300*


---

## Page 301

METHODS AND MATERIALS All molluscan pieces were examined by hand. Only those units that could be determined to represent an individual (i.e, bivalves = umbos, valves; gastropods = columella, apex, aperture) were counted. “Tooled” specimens were examined for smooth holes, unnatural fractures, rubbed edges, and any other factors that would suggest usage or consumption. Data were entered into a Microsoft Access database software program. TERMINOLOGY IN REPORT AND DATABASE Heat treated: A term used by the author to identify bivalves that have been heated in some fashion other than being placed directly in or over a fire. Valves that have been heated indirectly, such as in hot water or by steaming, will turn a dark, salmon color. Indirect heating changes the crystalline latticework of the carbonate molecules in the prismatic and nacreous layers of the valve. In contrast, a valve placed directly in a fire will char and turn a greyish black. Unheated valves found in archeological sites will show a chalky white color when broken. uu.f.: unidentifiable unionid valve or umbo fragment. Charred: Specimens directly burned. Coloration is dark gray to black. RESULTS One thousand three hundred and forty-one (1,341) molluscan units were identified in excavated samples from five prehistoric sites in the Dallas Floodway Extension area (i.e, 41DL318, 41DL319/357, ‘41DL337, 41DL338, and 41DL356). This total number includes 10 freshwater bivalve species (1,131 specimens), and six terrestrial gastropod species (210 specimens). The dominant gastropod species were Polygyra texasiana texasiana and Helicina orbiculata tropica. The most numerous bivalve species were Amblema plicata and Plectomerus dombeyanus. These two species also had the greatest number of charred or heat-treated specimens. Due to the proximity of the excavated sites, discussions relating to the environment and climatology of the area are discussed here. The terrestrial gastropod species assemblage suggests that the environment of the five tested sites was typical riparian woodlands with adjacent open, grassy areas. The prehistoric climate was very similar to today’s climate for the area, with hot, dry summers and relatively mild winters. All the excavated gastropod species occur in the immediate area today. Gastropod Species by Habitat Riparian terrace deposits in open grassy areas to partially wooded areas along streams: Rabdotus dealbatus dealbatus Polygyra texasiana texasiana Hawaiia minuscula Generally on wooded limestone outcrops and partially wooded bottomlands: Helicina orbiculata tropica Heavily forested woodlands with damp soil and decaying logs and vegetation: Strobilops texasiana Pupooides albilabris The freshwater bivalve species indicate a stream or river environment (almost certainly, the Trinity River). The river featured deep poo! areas alternating with relatively shallow riffle zones. The pool areas had muddy bottoms while the riffle areas had gravelly bottoms. This environment is suggested by both the samples from the sites on the modern Trinity River (i.e., 41DL318 and 41DL319/357) and those from the sites adjacent to the Wastewater Treatment Plant (i.e., 41DL337, 41DL338, and 41DL356). The presence of the finger-nail clam (Sphaerium striatinum) at one of the Wastewater Treatment Plant sites indicates nearby shallow bodies of water with emergent, grassy vegetation but with clear, flowing water. This species also indicates the possibility that at least some of the excavated bivalve specimens were flood deposited. E-3


![Image 1 - Page 301](assets/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing/00 Buysse 2000 - Dallas Floodway Testing_p301_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 301*

## Page 302

Deep water (pool) species: Amblema plicata Plectomerus dombeyanus Proptera purpurata Lampsilis teres Lampsilis hydiana Many of the project bivalves undoubtedly were utilized as food resources. A numberof specimens were either heat- treated or charred. Most were heat treated, indicating that the prehistoric inhabitants possibly utilized some kind of hot water or steam technique to cook the bivalves. The charred specimens were burned after the meat was cooked and eaten. Bivalve bodies will dissolve or significantly shrivel if heated in the shell over an open fire. Since the vast majority of the excavated bivalve specimens were subadults, a fairly constant collecting pressure was Bivalve Species by Habitat Riffle zone species: Tritogonia verrucosa Truncilla truncata Quadrula pustulosa Quadrula mortoni Sphaerium striatinum exerted on the nearby bivalve populations, both poo! and riffle species. ## Page 304

Usage of Shells as Tools or Ornaments No specimens examined indicated any type of usage as tools or ornaments. Discussion No heat-treated or charred specimens were identified in the samples from Features 1, 2, or 4. Although this site yielded only one charred specimen, eight species of bivalves were recovered. Flood deposition would seem to be the answer, but this situation does not occur in other nearby sites. Perhaps the inhabitants consumed raw clams as well as cooked clams. Site 41DL337 Site Mollusca GASTROPODA (3) Polygyra texasiana texasiana Rabdotus dealbatus dealbatus Helicina orbiculata tropica eM NN — Total BIVALVIA (6) Amblema plicata Quadrula mortoni Plectomerus dombeyanus Truncilla truncata Proptera purpurata Ale wwnv wo _ Total Heat-Treated or Charred Specimens THT-41, Unit 2, Level 5 (140-150 cm bs) Unidentifiable unionid fragments — Heat Treated Usage of Shells as Tools or Ornaments No specimens examined indicated any type of usage as tools or ornaments. Discussion This site yielded relatively few gastropod and bivalve species and numbers of specimens. Virtually all of the gastropod shells and bivalve valves that were identifiable were reduced to shell fragments and umbos. In particular, all of the THT-41 bivalve specimens examined were unidentifiable fragments. Interestingly, a number of these fragments showed signs of being heat treated.

Discussion A number of heat-treated or charred specimens (n=105) were identified in Feature 3 (THT-36) at depths of 170-180 cm bs, 180-190 cm bs, and 190-200 cm bs. The vast majority of these specimens (n=99, 94 percent) were recovered from 180-190 cmbs. Other heated specimens were identified in the samples from Unit 6, from 110 to 210 cm bs (n=79, including heated unidentifiable unionid fragments), with the majority coming from 160-190 cm bs (n=60, 76 percent). In Unit 7, 110 heat-treated specimens were identified at depths of 190-230 cm bs, with most of them at 220-230 cm bs (n=74, 67 percent). These data suggest that this site was periodically occupied over an extended period of time. E-9

SITE 41DL318 Trench 1—at cutbank of Trinity River. Depth(cm) SoilZone Description 0-15 Cc Brown to pale brown (10YR 5/3 to 6/3 dry) fine sandy silt; dense; massive; abundant fine rootlets; common open krotovina; clear to abrupt boundary 15-97/120 2A Dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty clay; very firm; fine blocky structure with silt and sand coats on ped faces; many fine vertical krotovina filled with dark gray clay; boundary clear, smooth, dips gently north and south in trench wall, and marked by pieces of chalky limestone 97/120-215 3Ab Very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay; very firm to slightly plastic; strong medium to coarse blocky with thick clay skins on peds; abundant fine krotovina filled with material from zone above, particularly in upper part; abundant dark decaying fine rootlets; few pieces of chalky limestone; few small whole gastropod shells; mussel shell in trench wall at depths of 140 and 181 cm; piece of fire cracked rock in trench wall at 155 cm; small bone fragments, tooth, and red (2.5YR 4/4) clay in back dirt from a depth of 170 to 180 cm; gradual to diffuse boundary 215-250 3Bbca Dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) clay; firm to plastic; fine blocky with clay skins on ped faces; common smail white or buff calcareous concretions; common small angular rock fragments; gradual to diffuse boundary 250-385 3Cca Dark grayish brown to brown (10YR 4/2 to 3/3) clay; plastic; fine blocky with clay skins on some ped faces; abundant small white or buff calcareous concretions; common dark decaying organic material with gray colored haloes; gradual boundary 385-395 4Cgca Gray (10YR 5/1) clay; plastic; medium blocky; common fine calcareous concretions; zone heavily mottled orange (10YR 5/8; up to 50% of zone) NOTE: zone sampled between the depths of 115 to 125 cm Trench 2—approximately 20 m southeast of Trench 1, separated from it by shallow slough, and 5-6 m northwest of deep stream channel cut Depth(cm) SoilZone Description 0-15 Cc Brown (10YR 5/3 dry) fine sandy silt with clay lenses; dense; massive; abundant ’ woody roots and rootlets; clear to abrupt boundary 15-150 2A Dark gray to dark grayish brown (10YR 4/1 to 4/2) sandy clay; fine blocky with clay skins on ped faces; abundant woody roots in upper 40 cm; common small angular rock fragments; common pieces of plastic, particularly in upper portion; clear boundary 150-195 3Abca Very dark gray (LOYR 3/1) clay; very firm; strong blocky with heavy clay skins on ped faces; few very fine calcareous concretions along rootlet traces which increase in number with depth; two large vertical krotovina (1 cm x 5 cm) filled with material from overlying zone and gastropod shell fragments; bone and mussel shell in trench wall at 150-152 cm, one angular rock in back dirt at 160-170 cm, and bone in back dirt at 190- 200 cm; gradual to diffuse boundary which is heavily bioturbated and marked by filled vertical krotovina 195-300 3Bbca Very dark grayish brown to dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2 to 4/2) slightly silty clay; firm to plastic; coarse blocky with clay, silt, and sand coats on ped faces; zone slowly lightens with depth; abundant calcareous concretions to pea size and few fine black FeMn concretions; gradual to diffuse boundary 300-370 3Cca Brown (10YR 5/3) clay; firm to plastic; common calcareous concretions, some filling rootlet holes; some orange Fe stain F-3

Trench 3—approximately 30 m east of Trench 1 along cutbank of Trinity River and 50 cm higher than Trench 1 Depth (cm) 0-15 15-185/200 185/200- 245 245-310 Soil Zone Cc 2C 3Abca 3Bbca Description Brown (10YR 5/3) fine silty sand; loose; massive; abrupt boundary Dark grayish brown to grayish brown (10YR 4/2 to 5/2) clay; zone disturbed and contains common plastic pieces and some copper wire; abundant woody roots in upper part; gradual contact with few small rounded gravels; boundary dips to north in trench wall Very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay; coarse blocky with heavy clay skins; common fine light gray, tan, and buff calcareous concretions which increase in number with depth; abundant orange colored decaying organic material; mussel shell fragment in trench wall at 230 cm, burned rock and mussel shell in back dirt; small filled channel 55 cm wide and at least 50 cm deep cuts through zone in center of trench wall; diffuse boundary Very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) clay; coarse blocky structure with clay skins on ped faces; common light gray calcareous concretions; common dark orange decaying organic material Trench 4—approximately 30 m northwest of Trench 1 on cutbank of Trinity River Depth (cm) 0-10/15 10/15-92 92-160 160-200 Soil Zone Cc 2A 3Abca 3Bbca Description Brown (10YR 5/3 dry) silty fine grained sand; loose; massive; common rootlets; boundary abrupt to clear and marked by pieces of green plastic and yellow “crime scene” tape Dark gray to dark grayish brown (10YR 4/1 to 4/2) silty clay; very firm; fine blocky structure; few blocks of very dark gray (10YR 3/1) clay mixed in zone giving it a mottled appearance; boundary gradual and bioturbated Very dark gray (1OYR 3/1) clay; coarse blocky with heavy clay skins on peds; few fine calcareous concretions; mussel shell at 127 and 140 cm, and red clay at 130; common fine rootlets; gradual to diffuse boundary Dark gray (10YR 4/1) clay as above with common fine calcareous concretions NOTE: paleosol sampled between depths of 125 to 140 cm Trench 14—approximately 12 m southeast of Trench 1 and 8 m west of north end of Trench 2 Depth (cm) 0-5 15-150 150-195 195-300 300-370 Soil Zone Cc 2A 3Abca 3Bbca 3Cca Description Brown (10YR 5/3 dry) fine sandy silt with clay lenses; dense; massive; abundant woody roots and rootlets; clear to abrupt boundary Dark gray to dark grayish brown (10 YR 4/1 to 4/2) sandy clay; fine blocky with clay skins on ped faces; abundant woody roots in upper 40 cm; common small angular rock fragments; common pieces of plastic, particularly in upper portion; clear boundary Very dark gray (10 YR 3/1) clay; very firm; strong blocky with heavy clay skins on ped faces; few very fine calcareous concretions along rootlet traces which increase in number with depth; two large vertical krotovina (1 cm x 5 cm) filled with material from overlying zone and gastropod shell fragments; bone and mussel shell in trench wall at 150-152 cm; one angular rock in back dirt at 160-170 cm, and bone in back dirt at 190- 200 cm; gradual to diffuse boundary which is heavily bioturbated and marked by filled vertical krotovina Very dark grayish brown to dark grayish brown (10 YR 3/2 to 4/2) slightly silty clay; firm to plastic; coarse blocky with clay, silt, and sand coats on ped faces; zone slowly lightens with depth; abundant calcareous concretions to pea size and few fine black FeMn concretions; gradual to diffuse boundary Brown (10 YR 5/3) clay; firm to plastic; common calcareous concretions, some filling rootlet holes; some orange Fe stain F-4

## Page 329

SITE 41DL319/357 Trench 5—at west edge of site 41DL357, at east edge of copse of trees east of deep stream channel cut Depth (cm) 0-57 57-120 120-210 210-360 Soil Zone C 2C 3Abca * 3BC Description Brown to pale brown (10YR 5/3 to 6/3 dry) clayey, silty fine grained sand; upper 20 cm loose, more sandy, and root bound with hint of lamination; massive; lower part more clay rich and heavily bioturbated with common krotovina filled with silt and decaying material (tree roots?); few fine woody roots; last 10 cm dark brown (10YR 4/3), has very abundant small white gastropod shell fragments and very small pink rock (?) fragments with pieces of red cloth and rusty metal; boundary abrupt and irregular Dark brown (10YR 3/3) silty sand grading with depth to yellowish brown (10YR 5/6) slightly silty sand; friable to firm; massive; zone heavily bioturbated with abundant filled krotovina which gives zone a mottled look, particularly upper part; abundant woody roots; some rusty decayed metal fragments; clear boundary Black to very dark gray (10YR 2/1 to 3/1) clay; very firm; fine to medium blocky with heavy clay skins; upper few cm “browner” in color; common fine woody roots, most whitened with calcareous deposits; some dark orange decaying material; some vertical sand filled krotovina in upper part; few fine calcareous concretions which increase in number with depth; very gradually lightens in color to very dark gray to very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/1 to 3/2) and becomes more plastic with depth with some silt and sand on ped faces; rusty metal and cloth fragments in back dirt to a depth of 190 cm; 1 bone fragment and some mussel shell in back dirt from 150-165 cm; gradual to diffuse boundary Dark grayish brown (LOYR 4/2) slightly sandy clay; firm to plastic; massive; some purplish black decaying organic material Trench 6—approximately 30 m east of Trench 5 on cutbank of Trinity River, site 41DL357 Depth (cm) 0-33 33-66 66-99 99-109/121 109/121- 220 220-250 Soil Zone Cc 2AC 4Abca 4Bbca Description Very pale brown (10YR 7/3 dry) silty sand; dense or loose; massive above dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty, sandy clay; firm; massive; above very pale brown (10YR 7/3 to 7/4) laminated silt; some orange Fe stain; few rootlets; boundary abrupt Dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty, sandy clay; firm; weak blocky with sand coats on ped faces; heavily bioturbated with abundant open vertical krotovina which are Fe stained dark orange and some sand filled; the cutbank exposure is whitened with calcareous deposits and contains rusty decayed pieces of metal and several pieces of white porcelain; boundary clear to abrupt Very dark gray to very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/1 to 3/2) clay; firm to very firm; fine blocky structure; few rootlets; common vertical krotovina filled with sand and silt; 1 piece of decayed rusty metal in trench wall at 75 cm; boundary abrupt and heavily bioturbated with abundant vertical krotovina filled with material from this zone penetrating into underlying zone Yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) clayey fine grained sand and brownish yellow (10YR 6/8) fine grained sand; friable; massive; heavily bioturbated with abundant vertical krotovina filled with dark material (insect burrows?); common open rootlet holes; zone thickens to west in trench wall; abrupt, highly irregular boundary Black to very dark gray (1OYR 2/1 to 3/1) clay; very firm; coarse blocky with heavy clay skins on ped faces; few fine light gray calcareous concretions; few small gastropod shells; upper 13 cm is slightly lighter in color being very dark gray to dark gray (1OYR 3/1 to 4/1) with common vertical sand filled krotovina; mussel shell in trench wall at 196 cm and red clay in back dirt at 200 cm; gradual to diffuse boundary Dark brown (10YR 3/3) clay as above but increasingly plastic F-5

upper portion of zone highly bioturbated; few purple decaying organics; few light gray calcareous concretions; mussel shell in trench wall at 110 and 120-130 cm; bone at ‘155 cm; common mussel shell in back dirt from 120-140 cm

dense to very firm; fine blocky; few small gastropod shells; common fine rootlets and few woody roots; bioturbated with common open fine krotovina and abundant vertical sand filled krotovina; clear to abrupt boundary Zone consists of alternating graded units of clay and silty fine grained sand; NOTE: sampled every 20 cm from 220 cm to base of trench

abundant fine open ‘ rootiet holes lined with white calcareous deposits; gradual to clear boundary Grayish brown to dark gray (LOYR 5/2 to 4/1) sandy clay; very firm; blocky with sand and silt coats on ped faces; red (SYR 5/8) clay and charcoal mixed with orange (1OYR 5/4) sand in krotovina (?) in floor of trench Trench 12—halfway between Trenches 7 and 11 or roughly 20 m east of Trench 7 along cutbank of Trinity River Depth (cm) 0-15 15-106 106-171 171-200 200-275 Soil Zone Cc 2A 2C 3AC 4Abca Description Very pale brown (10YR 7/4) silty fine grained sand; friable to firm; massive to weak fine blocky; upper portion of zone finely laminated sand and silt, bioturbated with few rootlets; basal portion visibly whitened with calcareous deposits, few rootlets, and bioturbated with common fine vertical krotovina filled with dark clay from underlying zones; ## Page 351

CURATED MATERIAL The archeological materials from this investigation are to be curated at the Texas Archeological Research Laboratory at the University of Texas at Austin. The following list of items enumerates the materials to be curated at this facility under Contract No. DACA63-96-D-0038, Delivery Order No. 0043 (GMI Project No. 1138-043) and Purchase Order No. DACW63-99-P-0241 (GMI Project No. 1241-001) with the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers, Fort Worth District: One copy of the Scope of Work for 1138-043 One copy of the Scope of Work for 1241-111 One copy of the Final Report Original field notes, maps, and records One acid-free copy of all field notes, maps and records One acid-free copy of each updated site form Black-and-white contact sheets, negatives, and photo data sheets Color slides and photo data sheets Original artifact analysis data sheets Acid-free copies of the original artifact analysis data sheets Computer diskette(s) containing the Access 97 data base files for artifact and analysis data One printout of each Access 97 data base file, including a code book for deciphering each data base Laboratory and Field inventories for all collected materials All collected artifacts and samples
